# DETAILED LEGAL ANALYSIS

═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

# SECTION I: FEDERAL TTB REGULATORY COMPLIANCE

The $1.2 billion acquisition of Artisan Spirits & Brewing Company, LLC (ASBC) presents material compliance requirements under the Federal Alcohol Administration Act and Internal Revenue Code administered by the Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau (TTB). This section analyzes permit transfer procedures for ASBC's five production facilities (three breweries, one distilled spirits plant, one winery), formula approval requirements affecting 42 spirits formulations, Certificate of Label Approval (COLA) re-application requirements for 280 approved labels, and tied house law compliance affecting 18 taprooms and Oregon self-distribution operations. The analysis identifies one **CRITICAL** regulatory exposure: an ongoing TTB investigation into ASBC's Single Barrel Bourbon product using toasted wood staves during aging, presenting $1.45 million to $2.66 million in probable financial exposure (70-80% likelihood of formula denial) based on controlling bourbon standards of identity and established TTB precedent. Permit transfer requirements create operational continuity risk requiring 90-120 day advance planning to avoid production shutdowns valued at $500,000 to $2 million per month of delayed approval.

## A. TTB Regulatory Framework and Jurisdiction

The TTB exercises plenary authority over alcohol beverage production, labeling, and distribution pursuant to the Federal Alcohol Administration Act (FAA Act), codified at 27 U.S.C. § 201 *et seq*., and Internal Revenue Code provisions governing distilled spirits (26 U.S.C. §§ 5001-5692), wine (26 U.S.C. §§ 5041-5392), and beer (26 U.S.C. §§ 5051-5417).¹ The agency's implementing regulations appear at 27 C.F.R. Parts 1-40, establishing comprehensive qualification requirements for permit holders, standards of identity for alcohol beverage products, labeling approval procedures, tax obligations, and three-tier distribution system compliance.² TTB jurisdiction extends to all aspects of ASBC's operations across five production facilities and 18 consumer-facing taprooms.

The FAA Act authorizes TTB to regulate industry members "engaged in business as a distiller, brewer, rectifier, blender, or other producer, or as an importer or wholesaler" of beverage alcohol.³ ASBC qualifies as an industry member operating under three distinct permit categories: brewer (three facilities producing 525,000 barrels annually), distiller (DSP-OR-20145 producing 100,000 proof gallons annually), and winery/meadery (Hood River, Oregon facility).⁴ Each permit category imposes distinct qualification requirements, operational restrictions, tax obligations, and compliance monitoring protocols that survive ownership changes only through formal TTB-approved transfer procedures.⁵

Recent TTB modernization efforts—including comprehensive labeling regulation reorganization (2020-2022), establishment of American Single Malt Whisky standards of identity (effective January 19, 2025), brewery qualification streamlining (2022), and winery permit modernization (2024)—demonstrate the agency's commitment to regulatory flexibility while maintaining strict adherence to statutory product standards and consumer protection mandates.⁶ Notably absent from this modernization wave: any amendment to bourbon whisky standards of identity codified at 27 C.F.R. § 5.143, which maintain absolute prohibition on adding "coloring, flavoring, or blending materials" to products designated as bourbon.⁷ This regulatory stasis creates material risk for ASBC's wood stave-finished bourbon production, as analyzed in Section I.C below.

## B. Permit Transfer Requirements: Non-Transferability and Successor Qualification Mandates

### 1. Federal Alcohol Administration Act Basic Permits

TTB basic permits issued under the FAA Act are categorically non-transferable by operation of federal regulation. The controlling provision, 27 C.F.R. § 1.44, provides: "No basic permit shall be leased, sold, or otherwise voluntarily transferred, and, in the event of such lease, sale, or other voluntary transfer, such basic permit shall automatically terminate thereupon."⁸

The regulation permits successor proprietors to continue operations for **30 days following ownership change** if a new permit application is filed within that 30-day window.⁹ This "grace period" provision states: "The appropriate TTB officer may permit temporary operation of the business pending final action on any application by a successor for a basic permit to replace the terminated permit if such application is filed within 30 days following the change in proprietorship."¹⁰ However, failure to file within 30 days results in immediate termination of permit authority and mandatory production cessation.¹¹

The ASBC acquisition involves complete ownership change triggering automatic termination of all existing ASBC basic permits (distillery, breweries, winery) unless successor permit applications are filed within 30 days of closing.¹² Production operations must cease if permits terminate due to late filing, creating revenue exposure of **$500,000 to $2 million per month** of production shutdown across ASBC's five facilities generating estimated $120 million to $150 million annual revenue.¹³

TTB requires updated ownership disclosure under 27 C.F.R. § 1.42 identifying all officers, directors, and persons holding 10% or more equity interest in the acquiring entity.¹⁴ The agency conducts background investigations of new ownership, examining criminal history, business history, tax compliance, and financial responsibility of control persons.¹⁵ Processing timelines for complete ownership change applications depend on application completeness, background investigation complexity, and TTB workload.¹⁶

### 2. Distilled Spirits Plant (DSP) Registration Transfer

ASBC's Portland distillery operates under DSP registration number DSP-OR-20145, producing 100,000 proof gallons annually.¹⁷ Section 19.116 of 27 C.F.R. requires that when a change in proprietorship occurs, "the new proprietor, before commencing operations, must qualify in the same manner as the proprietor of a new plant" by filing TTB Form 5110.41 (DSP Registration) with complete financial information, ownership structure, premises description, and operational plans.¹⁸

DSP bonds are calculated based on maximum tax liability for spirits in bond at any one time, with minimum bond of $1,000 and no statutory maximum.¹⁹ For ASBC's production volume, estimated DSP bond requirement totals **$250,000**, reflecting potential tax exposure for aging inventory and finished goods in bond.²⁰ TTB's published processing time data shows **81-day average processing time** for DSP registration applications versus TTB's internal 75-day service standard, indicating processing delays even for complete applications.²¹ The 81-day average reflects median performance across all DSP applications submitted during 2024; individual applications may process faster or slower depending on complexity, but the 81-day median provides realistic timeline expectation.²²

Recent TTB staffing reductions (13% workforce decrease reported February 2025) have increased processing times across all permit categories.²³ Industry reports indicate actual DSP processing times ranging from 60-120 days depending on application completeness and whether background investigations require additional information from applicants.²⁴ For ASBC's successor DSP registration, conservative timeline estimate is **81 days** based on TTB's published average, creating potential 51-day gap between permit termination (Day 30 if acquirer waits to file on the last day of grace period) and permit approval (Day 81), during which production must cease.

**Revenue exposure for 51-day distillery shutdown: $850,000 to $1.7 million** based on $15 million annual spirits revenue ($1.25 million monthly) and considering lost sales, distributor relationship damage, and production restart costs.²⁵

### 3. Brewery Permit Transfer Requirements

ASBC operates three brewing facilities requiring separate Brewer's Notice qualification under 27 C.F.R. Part 25:
- Portland, Oregon: 350,000 barrels annual capacity
- Denver, Colorado: 100,000 barrels annual capacity
- Asheville, North Carolina: 75,000 barrels annual capacity²⁶

Each facility requires new TTB Form 5130.10 (Brewer's Notice) filed by the acquiring entity.²⁷ While 27 C.F.R. § 25.71 permits amended Brewer's Notices for corporate changes affecting officers, directors, or stockholders holding 10% or more equity, complete changes in proprietorship require full requalification "in the same manner as the proprietor of a new brewery before commencing or continuing business."²⁸ The ASBC acquisition constitutes a complete proprietorship change triggering full requalification rather than mere amendment.²⁹

TTB processing timelines for brewery permits typically range 60-90 days for complete applications, though official statistics aggregate all permit types rather than publishing brewery-specific data.³⁰ The June 8, 2022 modernization of brewery qualification requirements extended certain reporting deadlines from 30 to 60 days (affecting post-approval amendments under 27 C.F.R. §§ 25.71, 25.74, 25.75) but did not modify initial qualification timelines.³¹

Each brewery permit requires surety bond coverage if annual tax liability exceeds $50,000.³² For brewers paying taxes semimonthly, bond amount equals 10% of annual tax liability.³³ ASBC's 525,000 total barrel capacity, taxed at $3.50 per barrel for the first 60,000 barrels and $16.00 per barrel thereafter (qualifying small producer rates under Craft Beverage Modernization Act), generates estimated annual federal excise tax of $7.63 million, requiring bond coverage of approximately $760,000.³⁴ However, individual facility bonds may be structured based on per-facility production, resulting in estimated $150,000 bonds for each of the three breweries ($450,000 total brewery bonds).³⁵

### 4. Winery/Basic Permit Requirements

ASBC's Hood River, Oregon winery/meadery requires new basic permit application under 27 C.F.R. Part 24.³⁶ Section 24.130 mandates that successor proprietors "before commencing operations" must "apply for and obtain any required permits" and "file any required bonds."³⁷ The outgoing proprietor (seller) must comply with transfer of materials requirements at 27 C.F.R. § 24.140, documenting all wine and spirits inventory, materials in production, and tax-determined wine in storage as of the transfer date.³⁸

Winery bonds are calculated based on tax exposure for all wine and spirits in transit or stored in the winery at any one time.³⁹ Given ASBC's specialty mead production (typically lower volume than beer or spirits), estimated winery bond requirement totals $50,000.⁴⁰ TTB's October 2, 2024 modernization of winery permit applications streamlined certain application requirements, potentially improving processing times, though no specific timeline data is published for winery permits alone.⁴¹

### 5. Aggregate Timeline and Operational Continuity Risk Assessment

**CRITICAL PATH ANALYSIS:**

| Permit Type | Facility | Processing Time | Grace Period Gap | Revenue at Risk |
|-------------|----------|----------------|------------------|-----------------|
| DSP | Portland OR | 81 days average | 51 days | $850K-$1.7M |
| Brewer's Notice | Portland OR | 60-90 days | 30-60 days | $3M-$6M |
| Brewer's Notice | Denver CO | 60-90 days | 30-60 days | $850K-$1.7M |
| Brewer's Notice | Asheville NC | 60-90 days | 30-60 days | $650K-$1.3M |
| Winery Permit | Hood River OR | 60-90 days | 30-60 days | $400K-$800K |
| **TOTAL EXPOSURE** | **5 facilities** | **60-90 days** | **30-60 days** | **$5.75M-$11.5M** |⁴²

The 30-day filing grace period provides insufficient buffer for TTB's current processing timelines. Even aggressive Day 1 filing (immediately upon closing) results in permit approvals on Days 60-90, creating potential 30-60 day production shutdowns at multiple facilities. If acquirer waits until Day 30 to file (the regulatory maximum), production shutdowns extend to 30-60 days at breweries and 51 days at the distillery, representing $5.75 million to $11.5 million in revenue exposure across all five facilities.⁴³

**Severity Rating: HIGH** (not CRITICAL, as mitigation strategies exist to minimize exposure).

**Probability Assessment:** 30-40% likelihood of production shutdown if standard post-closing permit filing timeline is followed; reduced to 5-10% if pre-closing conditional applications are filed 90-120 days before closing and TTB provides expedited review for ownership change transactions.⁴⁴

See Section I.G (Post-Closing TTB Actions), supra at ¶¶ 156-168, regarding permit application filing requirements within 30 days of closing as express closing condition precedent with seller cooperation covenant.

See Section IV.B.3 (Performance Requirements), supra at ¶¶ 47-81, analyzing how 60-90 day permit approval timelines affect distributor supply continuity under 305 active distribution agreements.

### 6. Surety Bond Pre-Qualification Requirements

Aggregate surety bond requirements across all five ASBC facilities total **$750,000**:
- DSP (Portland): $250,000
- Breweries (3 facilities): $450,000 ($150,000 each)
- Winery (Hood River): $50,000⁴⁵

Bond premiums range from 1% to 10% of bond amount depending on acquirer credit score, financial strength, bond type, and surety provider risk assessment.⁴⁶ For established corporate acquirers with strong credit profiles, premiums typically fall in the 1-3% range ($7,500-$22,500 annually); for highly leveraged acquisitions or private equity transactions, premiums may reach 5-10% ($37,500-$75,000 annually).⁴⁷ The $1.2 billion acquisition suggests substantial acquirer financial capacity, supporting lower-end premium estimates of $10,000-$25,000 annually assuming investment-grade or near-investment-grade credit profile.⁴⁸

Surety bonds must be in place before TTB issues permits, creating a prerequisite condition for operational continuity.⁴⁹ Pre-qualification with surety providers during the pre-closing due diligence period (60-120 days before closing) ensures bonds are ready for immediate issuance upon closing, eliminating surety underwriting delays from the critical path timeline.⁵⁰ Surety underwriting typically requires 14-30 days for financial review, credit analysis, and bond document preparation; advance preparation removes this delay from post-closing timeline compression.⁵¹

## C. Bourbon Standards of Identity: Toasted Wood Stave Investigation (CRITICAL RISK)

### 1. Regulatory Framework for Bourbon Whisky

Federal standards of identity for bourbon whisky appear at 27 C.F.R. § 5.143(c), relocated from former 27 C.F.R. § 5.22 during TTB's 2020-2022 labeling regulation reorganization (substantive requirements unchanged).⁵² Bourbon whisky is defined as whisky produced:

1. At not exceeding 160° proof from a fermented mash of not less than 51% corn grain;
2. Stored at not more than 125° proof;
3. In charred new oak containers.⁵³

"Straight bourbon whisky" requires storage in charred new oak containers for two years or more.⁵⁴ These production requirements are mandatory but not alone sufficient for bourbon designation. The critical regulatory prohibition appears in the standards of identity for whisky generally: **"No coloring, flavoring, or blending materials may be used in the production of spirits designated as 'bourbon whisky' in accordance with § 5.22(b)(1)(i) or 'straight' whisky."**⁵⁵

This prohibition is absolute. Unlike vodka (which may be treated with sugar and citric acid) or whisky specialties (which permit harmless coloring, flavoring, or blending materials), bourbon whisky admits zero additives beyond water reduction for bottling proof.⁵⁶ The regulatory text provides no exceptions, safe harbors, or de minimis thresholds. Any addition of coloring, flavoring, or blending materials categorically disqualifies a product from bourbon designation, requiring reclassification as a whisky specialty with mandatory label disclosures.⁵⁷

### 2. Treatment with Wood: Regulatory Classification as "Flavoring"

The term "coloring, flavoring, or blending material" explicitly includes wood treatment beyond standard barrel aging. Section 5.39(c) addresses "Treatment with wood," specifying that distilled spirits treated with wood (including oak chips, staves, or other wood additives) during distillation or storage—other than through normal contact with the oak container in which the spirits are stored—require label disclosure stating "colored and flavored with wood (insert chips, slabs, etc., as appropriate)" as part of the class and type designation.⁵⁸

TTB treats wood staves inserted into barrels during aging as "added HCFBM (harmless coloring, flavoring, or blending materials)" subject to formula approval requirements.⁵⁹ Products receiving wood treatment during aging must be labeled as whiskey specialties rather than bourbon, with front-label disclosure of the wood treatment process.⁶⁰ TTB allows producers to reference "bourbon" on specialty whiskey labels "as long as it is followed with the statement of what was done to it that made it no longer Bourbon," with this disclosure appearing on the front label.⁶¹ Example compliant labels include: "Bourbon Whiskey Finished with Oak Staves" or "Bourbon Whiskey - Colored and Flavored with Wood Staves."⁶²

### 3. Maker's Mark 46 Precedent: Definitive TTB Position on Wood Staves

The dispositive precedent for ASBC's bourbon formula issue is Maker's Mark 46, a premium whiskey product using **seared French oak staves** inserted into barrels during the aging process—a production method directly analogous to ASBC's Single Barrel Bourbon using **toasted wood staves**.⁶³ Despite being produced by one of the most prominent bourbon distilleries using traditional bourbon mash bill and aging in new charred oak barrels, Maker's Mark 46 is **not labeled as bourbon** and cannot be marketed as bourbon under TTB regulations.⁶⁴

Maker's Mark 46 is classified as a **"Whisky Finished with Oak Staves"** and falls under the whiskey specialty category at 27 C.F.R. § 5.39(c).⁶⁵ The product label explicitly discloses the wood stave finishing process, stating "Finished with French Oak Staves" as part of the class and type designation.⁶⁶ This classification reflects TTB's definitive position that wood staves—whether seared, toasted, or otherwise treated—constitute "added flavoring" prohibited under bourbon standards of identity.⁶⁷

The Maker's Mark 46 precedent is particularly instructive because:
- The base spirit qualifies as bourbon before stave insertion (≥51% corn, new charred oak aging, proper proof limits)
- The staves are oak (the same wood species required for bourbon barrels)
- The staves undergo heat treatment (searing) similar to barrel charring
- Maker's Mark is an established, well-capitalized distiller with sophisticated regulatory counsel⁶⁸

If TTB required Maker's Mark—a flagship bourbon brand with substantial regulatory expertise—to reclassify a stave-finished product as whiskey specialty, TTB will almost certainly apply the same standard to ASBC's toasted stave bourbon.⁶⁹ No published TTB rulings, industry guidance, or regulatory amendments since Maker's Mark 46's introduction suggest any relaxation of this classification requirement.⁷⁰

### 4. ASBC Single Barrel Bourbon: Factual Background

TTB received a competitor complaint in September 2024 alleging that ASBC's Single Barrel Bourbon product violates bourbon standards of identity by using **toasted wood staves** during the aging process to enhance flavor profile.⁷¹ The complaint alleges these staves constitute "added flavoring" explicitly prohibited under 27 C.F.R. § 5.143 for bourbon-designated products.⁷² TTB opened a formal investigation following the complaint, with determination expected during Q1 2026.⁷³

ASBC maintains that the toasted wood staves are part of the permitted "new charred oak barrel" aging process rather than separate added flavoring.⁷⁴ This position lacks regulatory support. The bourbon standard requires aging "in charred new oak containers"—a singular container requirement, not a hybrid container-plus-supplemental-wood approach.⁷⁵ The regulatory text provides no exception for additional oak contact beyond the container itself, and TTB's treatment with wood provisions at § 5.39(c) explicitly classify such supplemental wood contact as "flavoring" requiring special labeling.⁷⁶

Current Single Barrel Bourbon inventory totals **$4 million** at cost, representing finished goods inventory available for sale plus aging barrel inventory.⁷⁷ All units in this inventory were produced using the toasted wood stave method under investigation, creating uniform exposure across the entire product line.⁷⁸ ASBC filed TTB Form 5100.51 (Application for and Certification/Exemption of Formula) for the Single Barrel Bourbon formula, which TTB approved at the time of initial production.⁷⁹ The current investigation represents a potential formula approval revocation based on alleged misclassification of the product as bourbon rather than whiskey specialty.⁸⁰

### 5. Probability Analysis: Formula Denial Likelihood

**PROBABILITY ASSESSMENT: 70-80% likelihood TTB denies bourbon designation and requires reclassification as whiskey specialty.**⁸¹

This assessment derives from:

**Regulatory Text Analysis (80% weight):** The absolute prohibition on additives in 27 C.F.R. § 5.143 admits no exceptions. Wood treatment provisions at § 5.39(c) explicitly classify supplemental wood contact as "flavoring." TTB's 2020-2025 modernization efforts amended numerous labeling and qualification requirements but left bourbon standards completely unchanged, signaling no policy shift toward permissive interpretation of bourbon additives.⁸²

**Maker's Mark 46 Precedent (90% weight):** TTB's classification of Maker's Mark 46 as whiskey specialty rather than bourbon provides direct precedent for wood stave treatment. No evidence suggests TTB has reconsidered this classification or created any pathway for stave-finished products to retain bourbon designation.⁸³

**Absence of Contrary Authority (85% weight):** Research identified no published TTB rulings, private letter rulings, industry guidance documents, or enforcement actions supporting bourbon classification for wood stave-finished products. The regulatory silence favors strict enforcement of the written prohibition rather than permissive interpretation.⁸⁴

**Competitor Complaint Motivation (60% weight):** The September 2024 complaint suggests ASBC may have competitive advantage in the premium bourbon market based on the wood stave finishing process. Competitors' willingness to challenge the product classification indicates industry understanding that stave finishing violates bourbon standards. TTB's decision to open formal investigation (rather than dismissing the complaint) suggests preliminary agency assessment that the classification issue has merit.⁸⁵

**Policy Considerations (40% weight):** TTB's consumer protection mandate prioritizes accurate product labeling and standards of identity enforcement to prevent consumer confusion. Allowing ASBC to market wood stave-finished whiskey as bourbon while requiring Maker's Mark 46 to use whiskey specialty classification would create competitive inequity and regulatory inconsistency.⁸⁶

The 20-30% probability of formula approval rests entirely on the possibility that TTB distinguishes toasted staves from seared staves based on heat treatment intensity, finds ASBC's toasted staves qualify as part of the "charred new oak container" itself (as opposed to supplemental flavoring), or determines that prior formula approval estops revocation absent changed circumstances.⁸⁷ None of these theories finds support in regulatory text or precedent, rendering them speculative defenses rather than probable outcomes.⁸⁸

### 6. Financial Exposure Analysis

**SCENARIO 1: TTB Approves Formula (20-30% Probability)**

Financial Impact: $0
ASBC continues marketing Single Barrel Bourbon under current classification. No inventory write-down, no relabeling costs, no penalties. This outcome resolves all bourbon-related regulatory risk.⁸⁹

**SCENARIO 2: TTB Denies Formula, Requires Whiskey Specialty Classification (70-80% Probability)**

Financial Impact: $1.45 million to $2.66 million aggregate exposure⁹⁰

**Component 1: Inventory Write-Down ($520,000-$1.2 million)**

Bourbon commands premium pricing in the spirits market based on heritage, production requirements, and consumer perception. Industry data indicates bourbon-designated products command 30-50% price premium over whiskey specialty products of comparable age and quality.⁹¹ Reclassification of ASBC's Single Barrel Bourbon to "Whiskey Finished with Toasted Oak Staves" destroys this premium, requiring inventory valuation markdown of $520,000 (13% markdown assuming 30% price impact on $4 million inventory) to $1.2 million (30% markdown assuming 50% price impact).⁹² The actual markdown depends on acquirer's pricing strategy post-reclassification; aggressive discounting to clear inventory under new label accelerates revenue recognition but increases near-term write-down.⁹³

**Component 2: Relabeling Costs ($380,000-$880,000)**

TTB revocation of bourbon formula approval automatically invalidates all Single Barrel Bourbon COLAs, as the approved class and type designation no longer matches the regulatory classification.⁹⁴ ASBC must file new formula approval for "Whiskey - Colored and Flavored with Toasted Oak Staves" (27 C.F.R. § 5.39(c) designation) and file new COLAs showing the corrected class and type on all labels.⁹⁵

COLA re-application costs: $8,000-$12,000 (assuming 4-6 Single Barrel Bourbon SKUs requiring new TTB Form 5100.31 applications at approximately $2,000 legal/compliance cost per COLA).⁹⁶

Label printing and repackaging costs: $200,000-$400,000 (assuming $4 million inventory requires complete label replacement, with printing costs of 5-10% of wholesale product value).⁹⁷

Finished goods relabeling labor: $50,000-$100,000 (assuming 40,000-60,000 bottles in finished inventory requiring label removal and reapplication at $1.25-$1.65 per bottle labor cost).⁹⁸

Destruction of obsolete bourbon labels: $20,000-$30,000 (disposal costs and write-off of unusable label inventory).⁹⁹

Point-of-sale materials update: $100,000-$350,000 (neck hangers, shelf talkers, distributor sales sheets, website updates, promotional materials referencing "bourbon" designation must be withdrawn and replaced).¹⁰⁰

**Component 3: TTB Civil Penalties ($300,000-$580,000)**

The Federal Alcohol Administration Act authorizes civil penalties for labeling violations at 27 U.S.C. § 207. TTB exercises discretion in penalty assessment based on violation severity, duration, number of units in commerce, economic benefit to the violator, compliance history, and good faith efforts to remedy violations.¹⁰¹

For misbranding violations involving incorrect standards of identity designation, TTB typically assesses penalties ranging from $1,000 to $10,000 per violation, with each mislabeled product potentially constituting a separate violation.¹⁰² However, TTB typically aggregates violations for products from the same production run or formula approval, avoiding per-bottle penalties that could reach millions of dollars.¹⁰³

Estimated penalty range: $300,000-$580,000, reflecting:
- Material nature of bourbon vs. whiskey specialty misclassification (affects consumer purchasing decisions)
- Extended violation period (September 2024 complaint suggests ongoing sales for 12+ months)
- Competitor complaint demonstrates market-level consumer confusion
- $4 million inventory size indicates substantial volume in commerce
- Mitigating factors: prior formula approval suggests good faith reliance; no evidence of intentional misbranding; prompt corrective action upon determination¹⁰⁴

**Component 4: Lost Revenue During Product Recall/Relabeling ($250,000)**

Assuming 30-60 day period for COLA re-approval and relabeling of existing inventory, ASBC cannot sell Single Barrel Bourbon during this transition.¹⁰⁵ With $4 million inventory representing 6-12 months of Single Barrel Bourbon sales (depending on product velocity), estimated monthly revenue of $330,000-$650,000 suggests lost revenue of $250,000 during one month of complete sales suspension while relabeling occurs.¹⁰⁶ Distributor/retailer channel adjustments may extend market impact beyond the relabeling period as buyers adjust to new product classification.¹⁰⁷

**AGGREGATE EXPOSURE: $1.45 million to $2.66 million (70-80% probability scenario)**¹⁰⁸

**Probability-Weighted Expected Value: $1.02 million to $2.13 million** (applying 70-80% probability to aggregate exposure range).¹⁰⁹

[Cross-reference: Bourbon formula denial constitutes "known trend or uncertainty" under Item 303 of Regulation S-K requiring disclosure in 10-K/10-Q Management's Discussion and Analysis. Securities compliance analysis pending in Section II (not yet completed).]

See Financial Impact Analysis § II.B (TTB Regulatory Risk), at pp. 34-41, for Monte Carlo simulation incorporating bourbon formula exposure of $1.45M-$2.66M as discrete event risk with 70-80% probability.

See Section III.D (FET Assignment Optimization), supra at ¶¶ 219-320, analyzing tax implications of inventory write-down of $520K-$1.2M under I.R.C. § 165 and its effect on purchase price allocation.

### 7. Criminal Penalty Exposure (26 U.S.C. § 5687)

In addition to civil penalties under the FAA Act, criminal penalties apply under the Internal Revenue Code. Section 5687 provides that any person who violates provisions of Chapter 51 (Distilled Spirits, Wines, and Beer) or regulations issued pursuant thereto, for which no specific criminal penalty is prescribed, shall be:
- Fined not more than $1,000, or
- Imprisoned not more than one year, or
- Both, for each offense¹¹⁰

TTB rarely pursues criminal prosecution for standards of identity violations absent evidence of intentional fraud or consumer harm.¹¹¹ ASBC's prior formula approval and absence of evidence suggesting intentional misbranding make criminal prosecution unlikely (probability <5%).¹¹² However, the statutory maximum $1,000 fine per violation remains a theoretical exposure point for each improperly labeled product unit.¹¹³ With $4 million inventory potentially representing 40,000-80,000 bottles (depending on product mix of 750ml vs. 1L packaging), theoretical maximum criminal penalty exposure reaches $40-80 million if TTB pursued per-bottle prosecution—an extreme scenario without precedent in TTB enforcement history.¹¹⁴

More realistically, if TTB pursued criminal misdemeanor charges (again, highly unlikely given facts), the agency would treat the formula approval as a single violation or aggregate violations by production batch, resulting in penalties in the $1,000-$10,000 range.¹¹⁵ This analysis excludes criminal penalties from the quantified exposure calculation given <5% probability of criminal prosecution, but notes theoretical exposure for completeness.¹¹⁶

### 8. Additional Consequences Beyond Financial Exposure

**Permit Suspension Risk:** Serious or repeated labeling violations can trigger FAA Act basic permit suspension proceedings under 27 C.F.R. Part 1.¹¹⁷ Given ASBC's prior formula approval and single-product issue, permit suspension is unlikely for first-time bourbon classification violation.¹¹⁸ However, if acquirer continues producing and selling toasted stave bourbon under bourbon classification after receiving TTB's denial determination, escalated enforcement including permit suspension becomes probable.¹¹⁹

**Brand Reputation Impact:** Reclassification from bourbon to whiskey specialty affects brand positioning in the premium spirits market. Consumer perception associates bourbon designation with authenticity, tradition, and regulatory compliance; whiskey specialty classification may be perceived as inferior or non-traditional product.¹²⁰ Distributor and retailer reactions to forced reclassification present relationship management challenges and potential loss of premium shelf placement.¹²¹

**Competitive Market Impact:** The competitor complaint suggests industry awareness of ASBC's classification issue. TTB denial of bourbon designation may embolden competitors to highlight ASBC's "non-bourbon" status in marketing and sales pitches, creating competitive disadvantage.¹²² Conversely, if TTB approves ASBC's formula (20-30% scenario), ASBC gains competitive advantage as an approved wood stave bourbon producer while Maker's Mark 46 remains classified as whiskey specialty—an unlikely regulatory inconsistency.¹²³

## D. Certificate of Label Approval (COLA) Re-Application Requirements

### 1. COLA Non-Transferability Upon Ownership Change

TTB regulations define the COLA "certificate holder" as the permittee or brewer whose name, address, and basic permit number, plant registry number, or brewer's notice number appears on the approved TTB Form 5100.31.¹²⁴ Because all ASBC COLAs identify ASBC as the certificate holder with ASBC's permit information, these approvals cannot be transferred to the acquiring entity following the ownership change.¹²⁵

When proprietorship change occurs, "the certificate holder information (name, address, permit number) on the original COLA is no longer accurate," requiring the new proprietor to file new COLA applications with updated permit holder information.¹²⁶ This requirement extends to all **280 approved COLAs** in ASBC's product portfolio, covering beer labels (breweries), spirits labels (distillery), and wine/mead labels (winery).¹²⁷

TTB's COLA re-application requirement following ownership change serves important regulatory purposes:
- Verifies new permit holder qualifications and compliance status
- Ensures label accuracy reflecting current responsible party for regulatory violations
- Creates opportunity for TTB to review labels under current regulatory standards
- Establishes new certificate holder's responsibility for label compliance¹²⁸

Unlike permit transfers (which may be filed conditionally pre-closing), COLA applications require active permit status, creating sequential dependency: acquirer must receive permit approvals before filing COLA applications.¹²⁹ This sequence extends total timeline for full operational continuity to 120-180 days post-closing (60-90 days for permits plus 60-90 days for COLAs).¹³⁰

### 2. COLA Re-Application Process and Timeline

TTB accepts COLA applications via two methods:
- **COLAs Online electronic system** (https://www.ttb.gov/colasonline/) - preferred method, faster processing
- Paper TTB Form 5100.31 submission (duplicate copies required) - legacy method, slower processing¹³¹

Electronic submissions via COLAs Online typically process in **15-45 days** depending on application completeness, label complexity, formula approval status, and current TTB workload.¹³² Paper submissions experience longer processing times of 30-90 days.¹³³ For ASBC's 280 COLA re-applications, exclusive use of electronic submission is essential to minimize timeline exposure.¹³⁴

COLAs Online permits batch submissions, allowing acquirer to submit 20-30 COLA applications per week rather than processing individually.¹³⁵ With 280 total COLAs and batch submission capacity of 20-30 per week, initial submission phase spans 10-14 weeks (assuming organized preparation with all label artwork, formula approvals, and permit information compiled in advance).¹³⁶ Concurrent TTB review of batched applications yields approvals across 12-18 weeks following initial submission, assuming minimal rejections or requests for corrections.¹³⁷

**Aggregate Timeline Estimate:**
- Permit approvals: Days 60-90 post-closing (see Section I.B above)
- COLA preparation: Days 90-120 (concurrent with final permit approvals; label artwork compilation, formula verification, electronic application completion)
- COLA batch submission: Days 120-190 (10-14 weeks of rolling submissions)
- COLA approvals: Days 135-230 (15-45 days per COLA from submission date)
- **Full COLA portfolio approval: Days 180-230 post-closing (6-8 months)**¹³⁸

During the COLA re-application period, ASBC cannot use labels bearing acquirer's name and permit information, creating interim labeling challenge.¹³⁹ TTB may permit continued use of ASBC labels during transition period under the 30-day grace period provisions, but extended use beyond permit transfer completion date requires TTB approval.¹⁴⁰ Practical solution: negotiate with TTB for temporary label approval allowing sale of existing ASBC-labeled inventory while new COLAs process, avoiding production shutdown for labeling compliance.¹⁴¹

### 3. Financial Exposure Analysis

**Legal and Compliance Costs:**
COLA re-application requires attorney or compliance specialist review of each label for:
- Regulatory compliance with current 27 C.F.R. Parts 5, 7 (spirits, beer labeling)
- Mandatory information placement (brand name, class and type, alcohol content, health warning, net contents)
- Prohibited statements and misleading labeling
- Formula approval verification
- Certificate holder information accuracy¹⁴²

Estimated cost per COLA: $1,000-$2,000 depending on label complexity and whether formula modifications are required.¹⁴³

**280 COLAs × $1,000-$2,000 per COLA = $280,000-$560,000 total compliance cost.**¹⁴⁴

**Operational Impact:**
- New product launches delayed 6-8 months pending COLA approval¹⁴⁵
- Promotional activities referencing specific labels constrained during transition
- Distributor and retailer training on label changes
- Consumer confusion regarding package appearance changes¹⁴⁶

**Severity Rating: MEDIUM-HIGH** (not CRITICAL, as costs are quantifiable and timeline is manageable with planning, though aggregate cost of $280K-$560K represents material compliance burden).¹⁴⁷

**Probability Assessment: 100%** (COLA re-application is mandatory requirement, not a risk scenario).¹⁴⁸

### 4. Proposed Alcohol Facts Labeling Rule (Additional Future Exposure)

TTB published proposed rule on January 17, 2025, requiring "Alcohol Facts" nutrition panel on all alcohol beverage labels showing per-serving alcohol content, calories, carbohydrates, protein, and fat—similar to FDA Nutrition Facts panels on food products.¹⁴⁹ If this rule is finalized (expected late 2025 or 2026), effective date would likely allow 12-24 months for industry compliance.¹⁵⁰

**Impact if Finalized:**
All 280 COLAs would require amendment to add Alcohol Facts panel, triggering second round of COLA re-applications with estimated costs of $280,000-$560,000 (same cost structure as initial re-application).¹⁵¹

**Total potential COLA burden (transfer + Alcohol Facts): $560,000-$1.12 million** across 24-36 months post-closing.¹⁵²

**Probability Assessment for Alcohol Facts Rule Finalization: 60-70%** based on TTB's proactive consumer information policy objectives and absence of significant industry opposition during comment period.¹⁵³ However, implementation timeline provides adequate planning window (likely not required until 2027-2028), reducing immediate transaction impact.¹⁵⁴

**Recommendation:** Negotiate purchase agreement provision requiring Seller to represent and warrant that all COLAs comply with regulations in effect as of closing date, with Acquirer assuming compliance risk for regulatory changes occurring post-closing (including Alcohol Facts rule if finalized).¹⁵⁵ Acquirer should budget $280K-$560K contingency for potential Alcohol Facts compliance costs in 2027-2028 timeframe.¹⁵⁶

See Section I.H (Risk Quantification), supra at ¶¶ 169-195, for analysis of COLA re-application costs of $280K-$560K and purchase price adjustment recommendations.

## E. Tied House Law Compliance: Three-Tier System Requirements

### 1. Federal Tied House Prohibition Framework

The FAA Act Section 105(b)(3), codified at 27 U.S.C. § 205, prohibits producers and wholesalers from inducing retailers to purchase products to the exclusion of competitors—the anti-"tied house" provision designed to prevent vertical integration that dominated the pre-Prohibition alcohol industry.¹⁵⁷ TTB's implementing regulations at 27 C.F.R. Part 6 elaborate this prohibition, defining unlawful tied house practices including:

- Direct or indirect financial interest in retail establishments
- Furnishing, giving, renting, lending, or selling equipment, fixtures, signs, or supplies to retailers
- Extending credit beyond lawful limits
- Paying for advertising on behalf of retailers
- Providing free products beyond permissible samples
- Any inducement to purchase products to the exclusion of competitors¹⁵⁸

The regulations define "industry members" subject to these prohibitions as persons engaged in business as distillers, brewers, rectifiers, blenders, other producers, importers, or wholesalers of distilled spirits, wine, or malt beverages.¹⁵⁹ ASBC qualifies as an industry member operating production facilities, subjecting all ASBC operations to federal tied house restrictions.¹⁶⁰

The tied house prohibition serves three policy objectives:
1. **Competition Protection:** Prevents producers from controlling retail shelf space and consumer access
2. **Consumer Protection:** Ensures retailer independence in product selection serving consumer interests
3. **Tax Compliance:** Maintains clear separation between regulated tiers facilitating tax collection and enforcement¹⁶¹

Penalties for tied house violations include:
- Federal: Basic permit suspension or revocation, cease and desist orders, civil penalties under 27 U.S.C. § 207
- Criminal: Fines and potential imprisonment for willful violations
- State: License suspension/revocation, state fines, exclusion from state markets¹⁶²

### 2. On-Premise Consumption Exception: ASBC's 18 Taprooms

Federal regulations provide critical exception to tied house prohibitions for on-premise consumption at production facilities. Section 25.25 of 27 C.F.R. authorizes brewers to use brewery premises for other purposes upon TTB approval, including "operation of a tavern on brewery premises."¹⁶³ This exception permits producers to maintain 100% ownership interest in retail operations co-located with production facilities, selling directly to consumers without violating tied house prohibitions.¹⁶⁴

ASBC operates **18 taprooms** serving beer, spirits, and wine directly to consumers.¹⁶⁵ These taprooms likely qualify for the on-premise consumption exception if they are:
- **Co-located** with ASBC production facilities (same physical location or adjacent properties)
- Licensed as part of the brewery/distillery/winery premises rather than separate retail locations
- Operating under the production facility's TTB permit rather than separate wholesale/retail permits¹⁶⁶

**Critical Unknown:** The research materials do not specify whether all 18 taprooms are co-located with ASBC's five production facilities (3 breweries, 1 distillery, 1 winery/meadery) or whether some taprooms operate at separate locations distant from production.¹⁶⁷ If any taprooms operate as standalone retail locations not co-located with production:

- **Federal tied house issue:** Producer financial interest in off-premises retailer violates 27 C.F.R. Part 6
- **Remediation required:** Corporate restructuring to separate taproom ownership from production entities, or closure of off-premises taprooms
- **State law variation:** Some states permit producer-owned retail locations under specific statutory exceptions; state-by-state analysis required¹⁶⁸

**Severity Rating: LOW** (assuming all 18 taprooms are co-located with production facilities and qualify for federal exception).¹⁶⁹

**Probability Assessment:** 5-10% probability that one or more taprooms operate as off-premises retail locations creating tied house compliance issue; 90-95% probability all taprooms qualify for on-premise exception.¹⁷⁰

**Recommendation:** During due diligence, obtain:
- TTB license documentation for each taproom showing classification as on-premise retail or production premises extension
- Lease agreements or property records confirming co-location with production facilities
- State ABC licenses showing license classification for each taproom¹⁷¹

If any off-premises taprooms are identified, assess remediation cost (corporate restructuring legal fees estimated $50,000-$150,000) versus closure cost (lost revenue from affected locations).¹⁷²

### 3. Self-Distribution Exception: Oregon Direct-to-Retailer Sales

ASBC self-distributes **15% of Oregon production volume** (approximately 10,875 barrels annually based on 72,500 barrels total Oregon brewery production) directly to retailers without using third-party wholesaler intermediaries.¹⁷³ This practice raises tied house compliance questions under both federal and state three-tier system laws.

**Federal Law Analysis:**

Federal tied house regulations do not categorically prohibit brewery self-distribution.¹⁷⁴ The FAA Act treats brewers as manufacturers, and federal law authorizes limited self-distribution (direct sales to retailers without wholesaler intermediary) subject to state law restrictions.¹⁷⁵ Federal tied house prohibitions target producer financial interests in **retailers** and provision of things of value creating exclusivity arrangements; direct sales from producer to retailer constitute normal commercial transactions absent exclusivity inducements.¹⁷⁶

**State Law Dependency:**

Oregon state law governs whether ASBC's self-distribution complies with Oregon's three-tier system. State three-tier laws vary significantly:
- Some states follow federal model permitting brewery self-distribution for producers under specified volume thresholds
- Other states require strict three-tier separation with mandatory wholesaler intermediary
- Threshold limits vary (e.g., California permits self-distribution for breweries producing <60,000 barrels annually; Oregon limits unknown from research)¹⁷⁷

**Critical Unknown:** Research materials do not specify:
- Oregon statutory provisions governing brewery self-distribution
- Volume thresholds or production capacity limits for self-distribution qualification
- Geographic restrictions on self-distribution within Oregon
- Entity type restrictions (LLC vs. corporation vs. sole proprietor)
- Whether ASBC holds Oregon self-distribution license or operates under standard brewery license¹⁷⁸

**Risk if Oregon Law Prohibits Self-Distribution at ASBC's Volume:**

- **License Violation:** Operating without proper wholesale distribution license; OLCC (Oregon Liquor and Cannabis Commission) enforcement action
- **Civil Penalties:** Oregon administrative fines for three-tier violations
- **License Suspension:** Potential brewery license suspension during compliance remediation
- **Forced Transition:** Requirement to engage third-party distributor for all retail sales, losing 15% direct-to-retailer margin (estimated $500,000-$1 million annual margin impact based on approximately 10,875 self-distributed barrels × $25-40 per barrel margin advantage)¹⁷⁹

**Severity Rating: MEDIUM** (if Oregon law prohibits self-distribution at current volume, remediation requires operational restructuring with quantifiable margin impact).¹⁸⁰

**Probability Assessment:** 35-45% probability that Oregon law prohibits or restricts self-distribution at ASBC's current production volume, requiring remediation; 55-65% probability self-distribution is fully compliant under Oregon law.¹⁸¹ This assessment reflects:
- Most states permit limited brewery self-distribution below threshold limits
- Oregon's reputation as brewery-friendly regulatory environment
- Absence of evidence in research materials suggesting ASBC operates self-distribution unlawfully
- However, 350,000-barrel Portland brewery capacity may exceed typical small brewery self-distribution thresholds¹⁸²

**Recommendation:** Retain Oregon alcohol beverage counsel to provide legal opinion addressing:
1. Oregon statutory provisions governing brewery self-distribution
2. Production volume thresholds for self-distribution qualification
3. ASBC's compliance status under current operations
4. Remediation requirements if self-distribution exceeds authorized limits
5. Transition plan to third-party distribution model if required¹⁸³

Estimated legal opinion cost: $25,000-$40,000 for comprehensive Oregon three-tier compliance analysis.¹⁸⁴

See Section I.C (State Permit Coordination), supra at ¶¶ 89-112, analyzing Oregon state ABC permit transfer requirements concurrent with federal TTB permits.

### 4. Oregon OLCC Investigation: Taproom Equipment at Retailer Location

Research materials reference an Oregon OLCC (Oregon Liquor and Cannabis Commission) investigation concerning **taproom equipment at a retailer location**, suggesting potential tied house violation involving ASBC providing equipment to an independent retailer.¹⁸⁵ Federal tied house regulations prohibit producers from "furnishing, giving, renting, lending, or selling equipment, fixtures, signs, supplies" to retailers except under narrow exceptions.¹⁸⁶

**Factual Unknowns:**
- Nature of equipment provided (draft system, refrigeration, glassware, furniture)
- Value of equipment
- Terms of provision (loan, gift, sale at below-market price, lease)
- Whether retailer is independent third-party or ASBC-affiliated taproom
- Current status of OLCC investigation (pending, resolved, enforcement action taken)¹⁸⁷

**Potential Exposure:**

If OLCC determines ASBC violated Oregon tied house laws by providing equipment to independent retailer:
- **Civil Penalties:** Oregon administrative fines ranging $6,000-$60,000 for tied house violations depending on severity, violation history, economic benefit to violator¹⁸⁸
- **License Suspension:** 7-30 day brewery license suspension (avoided if ASBC enters settlement with corrective action plan)
- **Injunction:** Order to remove equipment from retailer premises and cease similar arrangements with other retailers¹⁸⁹

**Severity Rating: LOW** (assuming single-location violation with monetary penalties only, no license suspension).¹⁹⁰

**Probability Assessment:** 80-90% probability OLCC investigation results in administrative fine and corrective action; 10-20% probability investigation concludes no violation occurred.¹⁹¹

**Estimated Financial Exposure: $6,000-$60,000** (Oregon civil penalties plus equipment removal costs).¹⁹²

**Recommendation:**
1. Obtain from Seller: complete OLCC investigation file, all correspondence with OLCC, internal compliance review of equipment arrangements with all retailers
2. Assess whether equipment provision practice extends to multiple Oregon retailers (creating multiplied exposure)
3. Negotiate Seller retention of OLCC investigation liability in purchase agreement, with indemnification cap at $100,000 (covering fines plus settlement costs)
4. Implement post-closing tied house compliance audit across all states to identify and remediate similar arrangements¹⁹³

## F. Risk Assessment Matrix

| Risk Factor | Severity | Probability | Exposure Range | Timing | Mitigation Strategy |
|-------------|----------|-------------|----------------|--------|---------------------|
| **Bourbon Formula Denial** | **CRITICAL** | **70-80%** | **$1.45M-$2.66M** | Q1 2026 | Request expedited TTB determination; $2.66M escrow; halt production pending approval |
| **Permit Transfer Delays** | **HIGH** | 30-40% | $5.75M-$11.5M revenue loss | Days 30-90 post-closing | File applications 90-120 days pre-closing; expedited TTB review; 30-day filing guarantee |
| **COLA Re-Application Burden** | MEDIUM-HIGH | 100% (certain) | $280K-$560K | 6-8 months post-closing | Electronic COLAs Online filing; batch submissions; Seller artwork/formula support |
| **Bond Premium Costs** | MEDIUM | 100% (certain) | $10K-$25K annually | At closing | Pre-qualify surety; lock premium rates; demonstrate acquirer creditworthiness |
| **Oregon Self-Distribution** | MEDIUM | 35-45% | $500K-$1M annual margin loss | Post-closing | Oregon counsel legal opinion; transition plan to third-party distribution if required |
| **OLCC Tied House Investigation** | LOW | 80-90% | $6K-$60K | Ongoing | Seller indemnification; settlement with corrective action; compliance audit |
| **Off-Premises Taproom** | LOW | 5-10% | $50K-$150K restructuring | Post-closing | License verification; corporate restructuring if needed |
| **Alcohol Facts Labeling** | MEDIUM | 60-70% | $280K-$560K | 2027-2028 | Monitor Federal Register; budget contingency; plan 12-18 month COLA amendment timeline |

**Aggregate Quantified Exposure: $7.27 million to $15.1 million** across all risk categories (probability-unadjusted).¹⁹⁴

**Probability-Weighted Aggregate Exposure: $2.38 million to $4.62 million** (applying individual probabilities to each risk category and summing expected values).¹⁹⁵

See Section I.I (Escrow Recommendations), supra at ¶¶ 196-218, for detailed analysis of $3 million TTB regulatory escrow allocation with 18-month release schedule.

## G. Draft Remediation Language

### 1. Purchase Agreement - Bourbon Formula Provisions

**ARTICLE V - REPRESENTATIONS AND WARRANTIES OF SELLER**

**Section 5.19 - TTB Regulatory Compliance**

(a) **Formula Approvals.** Schedule 5.19(a) lists all distilled spirits formula approvals granted to Seller by the Alcohol and Tobacco Tax and Trade Bureau, including TTB Form 5100.51 approval number and date for each formula. All such formula approvals were validly obtained and remain in full force and effect as of the Closing Date. Seller has received no written or oral communication from TTB questioning, investigating, or proposing to revoke any formula approval listed on Schedule 5.19(a).

(b) **Bourbon Standards of Identity.** Seller represents that all products designated and labeled as "bourbon whisky" or "straight bourbon whisky" (collectively, "**Bourbon Products**") comply with the standards of identity for bourbon whisky as defined in 27 C.F.R. § 5.143, including without limitation the prohibition on adding coloring, flavoring, or blending materials to bourbon. Seller has received no written or oral communication from TTB or any competitor, industry association, or consumer alleging that any Bourbon Product fails to comply with bourbon standards of identity or constitutes a misbranded product under the Federal Alcohol Administration Act.

(c) **Single Barrel Bourbon Investigation.** Notwithstanding the representation in Section 5.19(b), Seller acknowledges that in September 2024, TTB opened an investigation concerning whether Seller's Single Barrel Bourbon product (which uses toasted wood staves during aging) complies with bourbon standards of identity at 27 C.F.R. § 5.143. As of the date of this Agreement, TTB has not issued any preliminary or final determination regarding the Single Barrel Bourbon formula approval. Seller has provided to Purchaser complete copies of all communications with TTB concerning the Single Barrel Bourbon investigation, all communications with the complainant (if known to Seller), and all analyses conducted by Seller or its counsel regarding the regulatory status of the Single Barrel Bourbon formula. Current Single Barrel Bourbon inventory totals $4,000,000 at cost as of [Due Diligence Date].

**ARTICLE VIII - COVENANTS**

**Section 8.9 - TTB Bourbon Determination**

(a) **Expedited Determination Request.** Within ten (10) Business Days following the date of this Agreement, Seller shall submit to TTB's Formulations Division a written request for expedited determination regarding the Single Barrel Bourbon formula approval, requesting final determination no later than thirty (30) days prior to the anticipated Closing Date. Seller shall provide Purchaser with a copy of such request and all subsequent TTB correspondence regarding the expedited determination.

(b) **Production Cessation.** Effective as of the date of this Agreement, Seller shall immediately cease all production of Single Barrel Bourbon using toasted wood staves until such time as: (i) TTB issues written determination approving the Single Barrel Bourbon formula under 27 C.F.R. § 5.143; or (ii) the Closing occurs. Seller may continue selling existing Single Barrel Bourbon inventory during the period prior to Closing.

(c) **Cooperation.** Seller shall cooperate fully with all TTB inquiries regarding the Single Barrel Bourbon investigation, including providing any requested production records, stave supplier information, barrel aging documentation, or other materials TTB may require to complete its investigation. Seller shall promptly provide Purchaser with copies of all materials submitted to TTB and all TTB responses.

**ARTICLE IX - CONDITIONS TO CLOSING**

**Section 9.2 - Conditions to Obligations of Purchaser**

The obligations of Purchaser to consummate the Closing are subject to satisfaction (or waiver by Purchaser) of the following conditions:

(h) **TTB Bourbon Determination.** Either: (i) TTB has issued written determination approving Seller's Single Barrel Bourbon formula under bourbon standards of identity at 27 C.F.R. § 5.143, with such approval delivered to Purchaser no less than ten (10) Business Days prior to Closing; or (ii) the Bourbon Escrow arrangements set forth in Section 10.3 are in full force and effect; or (iii) Seller has agreed to purchase price reduction in the amount of $2,660,000 as provided in Section 2.4(c).

**ARTICLE X - INDEMNIFICATION**

**Section 10.3 - Bourbon Formula Escrow**

(a) **Escrow Establishment.** At Closing, Seller shall deposit $2,660,000 (the "**Bourbon Escrow Amount**") with [Escrow Agent Name], to be held in escrow pursuant to the Escrow Agreement substantially in the form attached as Exhibit E (the "**Bourbon Escrow Agreement**"). The Bourbon Escrow Amount shall serve as security for Seller's indemnification obligations under Section 10.2(b) regarding Single Barrel Bourbon formula denial.

(b) **Escrow Release Conditions.** The Bourbon Escrow Amount shall be released as follows:

(i) **Formula Approval Release.** If, within eighteen (18) months following the Closing Date, TTB issues written determination approving the Single Barrel Bourbon formula under bourbon standards of identity at 27 C.F.R. § 5.143, the entire Bourbon Escrow Amount shall be released to Seller within ten (10) Business Days following delivery of TTB's written approval to the Escrow Agent.

(ii) **No Determination Release.** If, as of the date that is eighteen (18) months following the Closing Date, TTB has not issued any written determination (approval or denial) regarding the Single Barrel Bourbon formula, the Bourbon Escrow Amount shall be released to Seller within ten (10) Business Days, provided that no TTB investigation or enforcement proceeding concerning Single Barrel Bourbon remains pending as of such date.

(iii) **Formula Denial - Application to Losses.** If, at any time within eighteen (18) months following the Closing Date, TTB issues written determination denying the Single Barrel Bourbon formula approval under bourbon standards of identity or requiring reclassification of Single Barrel Bourbon as a whiskey specialty product, Purchaser may submit claims against the Bourbon Escrow Amount for all Losses arising from or relating to such denial, including without limitation: inventory write-downs, relabeling costs, TTB civil penalties, lost revenue during product recall/relabeling period, and legal fees incurred in responding to TTB enforcement actions. Any portion of the Bourbon Escrow Amount remaining after satisfaction of all Purchaser claims shall be released to Seller.

(c) **Indemnification Cap.** Seller's maximum aggregate indemnification obligation for all claims arising from or relating to Single Barrel Bourbon formula denial shall not exceed the Bourbon Escrow Amount ($2,660,000), and Purchaser's sole and exclusive remedy for such claims shall be recovery from the Bourbon Escrow Amount. Seller shall have no obligation to fund any Losses exceeding the Bourbon Escrow Amount.

**Section 10.4 - Permit Transfer Support**

Seller covenants and agrees that, for a period of one hundred eighty (180) days following the Closing Date:

(a) **Personnel Retention.** Seller shall cause the following key personnel to remain available to Purchaser to support TTB permit transfer process: (i) Master Distiller [Name]; (ii) Head Brewer - Portland [Name]; (iii) Head Brewer - Denver [Name]; (iv) Head Brewer - Asheville [Name]; and (v) Winemaker [Name]. Such personnel shall provide reasonable assistance to Purchaser in preparing TTB permit applications, responding to TTB inquiries, and maintaining operational continuity during permit transfer period. Seller shall compensate such personnel during the retention period, with Purchaser reimbursing Seller for fifty percent (50%) of salary and benefits costs for such personnel allocable to permit transfer support activities.

(b) **TTB Correspondence.** Seller shall provide Purchaser with copies of all TTB permit files, compliance records, inspection reports, and correspondence for all five (5) ASBC production facilities within ten (10) Business Days following the Closing Date.

(c) **Surety Bond Assistance.** Seller shall provide Purchaser's surety providers with all financial information, production records, and compliance documentation reasonably requested to facilitate surety bond underwriting and issuance of $750,000 aggregate surety bonds required for TTB permit transfer.

### 2. Interim Operating Covenant - Production Continuity

**ARTICLE VIII - COVENANTS**

**Section 8.10 - TTB Permit Application Pre-Filing**

(a) **Conditional Applications.** Subject to TTB acceptance of conditional applications pending transaction closing, Seller and Purchaser shall jointly prepare and file applications for new TTB permits for all five (5) ASBC production facilities no later than ninety (90) days prior to the anticipated Closing Date. Such applications shall be expressly conditioned upon consummation of the Closing and shall identify Purchaser as the successor proprietor. Seller shall cooperate fully in preparing such applications, including providing all required financial information, operational data, and compliance history documentation.

(b) **Early Filing Alternative.** If TTB declines to accept conditional permit applications pursuant to Section 8.10(a), Purchaser shall prepare all permit applications during the sixty (60) day period prior to Closing, with Seller providing all information and documentation reasonably requested by Purchaser. Purchaser shall file all five (5) permit applications with TTB within five (5) Business Days following the Closing Date, utilizing expedited filing procedures if available from TTB.

(c) **TTB Consultation.** Seller and Purchaser shall jointly engage in pre-filing consultation with TTB National Revenue Center no later than one hundred twenty (120) days prior to anticipated Closing Date to: (i) confirm permit application requirements; (ii) identify any potential application deficiencies; (iii) request expedited processing for ownership change transaction; and (iv) clarify grace period procedures for operational continuity during permit processing.

### 3. COLA Re-Application Support Covenant

**ARTICLE VIII - COVENANTS**

**Section 8.11 - COLA Re-Application Assistance**

(a) **Label Artwork Delivery.** Within ten (10) Business Days following the Closing Date, Seller shall deliver to Purchaser, in electronic format compatible with TTB COLAs Online system, complete label artwork files for all two hundred eighty (280) approved Certificates of Label Approval listed on Schedule 5.19(c), including front labels, back labels, neck labels, and all associated graphics and text in editable format.

(b) **Formula Documentation.** Concurrently with label artwork delivery under Section 8.11(a), Seller shall deliver to Purchaser copies of all TTB formula approvals (TTB Form 5100.51) for spirits products requiring formula approval, including all correspondence with TTB regarding formula applications, amendments, or clarifications.

(c) **Employee Support.** For a period of ninety (90) days following the Closing Date, Seller shall cause [Regulatory Compliance Manager Name] to provide reasonable assistance to Purchaser in preparing and filing COLA re-applications, including: (i) answering Purchaser questions regarding label compliance history; (ii) identifying formula approval dependencies for specific labels; (iii) reviewing draft COLA applications for accuracy; and (iv) communicating with TTB regarding any inquiries concerning historical COLA files. Seller shall compensate such employee during the retention period, with Purchaser reimbursing Seller for actual time spent providing COLA assistance at a rate of $150 per hour.

(d) **Budget Allocation.** Purchaser acknowledges that COLA re-application for 280 approved labels will require aggregate compliance and legal costs of approximately $280,000 to $560,000 over a six (6) to eight (8) month period following permit transfer approval. Seller makes no representation regarding the sufficiency of this budget estimate and shall have no liability for any costs exceeding these estimates.

## H. Implementation Recommendations

### Immediate Actions (Days -120 to -90 Pre-Closing)

**Priority 1 (CRITICAL): Bourbon Formula Determination**
- **Action:** Submit written request to TTB Formulations Division requesting expedited determination on Single Barrel Bourbon formula approval
- **Contact:** TTB National Revenue Center, Formulations Division: (202) 453-2250
- **Materials Required:** Complete production records for Single Barrel Bourbon, toasted stave specifications and sourcing, current formula approval documentation, competitor complaint (if available via FOIA)
- **Timeline:** Request final TTB determination within 30 days of request; offer to supplement with any additional information TTB requires
- **Fallback:** If TTB declines expedited determination, negotiate $2.66 million escrow fund in purchase agreement with 18-month release schedule¹⁹⁶

**Priority 2 (HIGH): TTB Permit Pre-Filing Consultation**
- **Action:** Schedule conference call with TTB National Revenue Center to discuss permit transfer timeline, application requirements, and potential for conditional pre-closing filing
- **Participants:** Acquirer counsel, Seller operations personnel, TTB permit specialist
- **Topics:** Expedited processing availability for ownership change, grace period operational procedures, application completeness checklist
- **Timeline:** Complete consultation 120 days before anticipated closing date¹⁹⁷

**Priority 3 (HIGH): Surety Bond Pre-Qualification**
- **Action:** Engage surety broker to pre-qualify $750,000 aggregate bond capacity ($250K DSP, $450K breweries, $50K winery)
- **Materials Required:** Acquirer financial statements, ownership structure, management backgrounds, ASBC historical tax compliance records
- **Deliverable:** Binding surety commitment letters with locked premium rates (target 1-3% = $7,500-$22,500 annually)
- **Timeline:** Complete surety underwriting 90 days before closing to ensure bonds ready for immediate issuance¹⁹⁸

**Priority 4 (MEDIUM): Oregon Three-Tier Compliance Opinion**
- **Action:** Retain Oregon alcohol beverage counsel to provide legal opinion on self-distribution permissibility for ASBC's production volume
- **Scope:** Oregon statutory authority for brewery self-distribution, volume thresholds, geographic restrictions, ASBC compliance status, remediation requirements if non-compliant
- **Cost Estimate:** $25,000-$40,000 for comprehensive opinion
- **Timeline:** Complete opinion 90 days before closing to allow transaction structuring adjustments if remediation required¹⁹⁹

### Pre-Closing Actions (Days -90 to Closing)

**Priority 5 (HIGH): Conditional Permit Applications (if TTB permits)**
- **Action:** Prepare and file all five TTB permit applications conditional on closing
- **Forms Required:**
  - 3× TTB Form 5130.10 (Brewer's Notice) - Portland, Denver, Asheville
  - 1× TTB Form 5110.41 (DSP Registration) - Portland
  - 1× TTB Form 5100.6 (Application for Basic Permit Under Federal Alcohol Administration Act) - Wine premises
- **Supporting Documents:** Ownership structure, financial statements, background questionnaires for all officers/directors/10%+ shareholders, premises descriptions, equipment lists, production process descriptions
- **Timeline:** File 90-120 days before closing if conditional filing permitted; otherwise, prepare applications for Day 1 filing²⁰⁰

**Priority 6 (MEDIUM): OLCC Investigation Resolution**
- **Action:** Obtain complete file of Oregon OLCC investigation regarding taproom equipment at retailer location
- **Assessment:** Determine potential penalty exposure, evaluate settlement options, negotiate Seller retention of liability
- **Purchase Agreement Provision:** Seller indemnification for OLCC investigation with $100,000 cap (covers $6K-$60K fine estimate plus settlement/legal costs)
- **Timeline:** Resolve or allocate liability before closing²⁰¹

**Priority 7 (MEDIUM): Taproom License Verification**
- **Action:** Review TTB and state ABC licenses for all 18 taprooms to confirm classification as on-premise retail vs. off-premises retail
- **Analysis:** Verify co-location with production facilities; identify any standalone taproom locations creating potential tied house issue
- **Remediation:** If off-premises taprooms identified, assess corporate restructuring cost ($50K-$150K) vs. closure cost
- **Timeline:** Complete verification 60 days before closing²⁰²

### Post-Closing Actions (Days 0-30)

**Priority 8 (CRITICAL): TTB Permit Application Filing**
- **Action:** If conditional pre-closing filing not completed, file all five permit applications within five (5) business days of closing (well within 30-day grace period)
- **Filing Method:** Electronic filing via TTB's Permits Online system (faster than paper) at https://www.ttb.gov/permits/permits-online.shtml
- **Follow-Up:** Monitor application status weekly; respond to TTB information requests within 48 hours; maintain communication with assigned TTB permit specialist
- **Timeline:** Target permit approvals Days 60-90 post-closing (DSP 81 days, breweries/winery 60-90 days)²⁰³

**Priority 9 (HIGH): Bourbon Production Cessation**
- **Action:** Immediately halt all Single Barrel Bourbon production using toasted wood staves pending TTB formula determination
- **Rationale:** Continued production during investigation increases inventory at risk and may be viewed negatively by TTB if formula ultimately denied
- **Alternative:** Continue producing bourbon WITHOUT toasted wood staves (traditional bourbon process), creating compliant inventory for sale during investigation period
- **Timeline:** Maintain production halt until Q1 2026 TTB determination or earlier if expedited approval received²⁰⁴

### Post-Closing Actions (Days 30-180)

**Priority 10 (HIGH): COLA Preparation and Filing**
- **Action:** Beginning Day 60 (concurrent with anticipated permit approvals), commence COLA re-application preparation
- **Workflow:**
  - Days 60-90: Compile all 280 label artwork files, verify formula approvals, prepare electronic applications in COLAs Online system
  - Days 90-190: Submit batches of 20-30 COLA applications per week (10-14 weeks of rolling submissions)
  - Days 105-230: Receive COLA approvals on rolling basis (15-45 days per application)
- **Resource Allocation:** Assign dedicated regulatory compliance specialist to COLA project; budget $280K-$560K for legal review and compliance costs
- **Timeline:** Achieve full 280 COLA approvals by Day 230 (approximately 8 months post-closing)²⁰⁵

**Priority 11 (MEDIUM): Three-Tier Compliance Audit**
- **Action:** Conduct comprehensive tied house compliance audit across all states where ASBC operates (Oregon, Colorado, North Carolina, plus any states where taprooms or distribution occurs)
- **Scope:** Review all retailer relationships, equipment provisions, promotional activities, credit terms, advertising support, sampling programs
- **Deliverable:** Compliance findings report with remediation recommendations for any identified violations
- **Cost Estimate:** $50,000-$100,000 for multi-state audit by alcohol beverage compliance consultants
- **Timeline:** Complete audit Days 0-90 post-closing; implement remediation Days 90-180²⁰⁶

### Long-Term Monitoring (Months 6-24)

**Priority 12 (MEDIUM): Alcohol Facts Labeling Monitoring**
- **Action:** Monitor Federal Register for final rule on Alcohol Facts labeling (proposed January 17, 2025)
- **Probability:** 60-70% likelihood rule is finalized, likely effective 2027-2028
- **Financial Impact:** If finalized, $280K-$560K for amending all 280 COLAs to add nutrition panel
- **Planning:** Budget $280K-$560K contingency for 2027-2028; plan 12-18 month COLA amendment timeline if rule finalized; participate in TTB comment process if rule weaknesses identified²⁰⁷

## I. Conclusion

Federal TTB regulatory compliance requirements for the ASBC acquisition present one CRITICAL risk (bourbon formula investigation, $1.45M-$2.66M exposure at 70-80% probability), two HIGH risks (permit transfer operational continuity, $5.75M-$11.5M revenue exposure at 30-40% probability if not properly managed; COLA re-application burden, $280K-$560K certain cost), and multiple MEDIUM and LOW risks aggregating to $2.38 million to $4.62 million probability-weighted expected exposure across all categories. With structured pre-closing planning—including expedited bourbon formula determination request, 90-120 day advance permit filing, surety bond pre-qualification, and Oregon three-tier compliance opinion—these risks are manageable within the scope of a $1.2 billion acquisition. The bourbon formula issue represents genuine regulatory uncertainty requiring either pre-closing TTB resolution or substantial escrow protection ($2.66 million for 18 months) to allocate risk appropriately between parties. All other TTB compliance matters are procedural requirements with quantifiable costs and timelines amenable to detailed implementation planning and purchase agreement covenant structuring.

123. Regulatory inconsistency scenario: if TTB approves ASBC's stave-finished bourbon while Maker's Mark 46 remains classified as whiskey specialty, Maker's Mark would likely seek reclassification creating industry-wide precedent for stave-finished bourbons; alternatively, TTB would need to explain distinction between toasted vs. seared staves—a technical differentiation lacking support in regulatory text. *Regulatory Rulemaking Analyst Report* at 58.
