## IV. COMMERCIAL CONTRACTS & DISTRIBUTOR NETWORK

ASBC operates a complex three-tier distribution network comprising 305 distributor relationships spanning all 50 states—180 beer distributors and 125 spirits distributors. This section analyzes the contractual architecture governing these relationships, the impact of 25+ state franchise laws that override contractual termination provisions, and the critical transaction risks posed by change-of-control consent requirements affecting an estimated 183-244 distributor agreements. The analysis reveals that ABP's anticipated post-acquisition consolidation strategy faces insurmountable legal and economic barriers totaling $217M-$558M, necessitating abandonment in favor of a "Grow Into" model that reduces exposure by $110M-$282M.

### A. Distributor Network Overview and Strategic Significance

ASBC's distribution infrastructure exhibits significant concentration risk, with the Top 4 distributors—Republic National Distributing Company (RNDC), Southern Glazer's Wine & Spirits (SGWS), Reyes Beverage Group, and Ben E. Keith—controlling 52% of total volume (18%, 14%, 12%, and 8% respectively).¹ The Top 10 distributors collectively represent 72% of sales volume, creating a critical dependency on relationships with these major wholesale partners.² Average relationship duration of 8.3 years indicates established market presence but also embedded contractual complexity as agreements have evolved through multiple amendment cycles.

Geographic coverage encompasses all 50 states for beer distribution and 48 states for spirits (excluding Utah and Mississippi control states where government agencies serve as exclusive distributors).³ This footprint operates within the mandatory three-tier system enforced by state alcohol beverage control laws, which constitutionally prohibits supplier-to-retailer direct sales except in narrow circumstances.⁴

The practical significance of this network structure cannot be overstated: **distributors own customer relationships, control retail account access, and possess statutory protections that effectively convert distribution rights into vested property interests.** Unlike typical commercial distribution agreements subject to contract law's freedom-to-terminate principles, beer franchise laws in 25+ states impose "good cause" requirements that render termination provisions unenforceable regardless of contract language.⁵

**Strategic Challenge for ABP:** The acquiring entity's anticipated consolidation from 305 distributors to 50 "strategic partners" directly conflicts with statutory regimes designed to protect incumbent distributors from precisely this type of supplier-driven network rationalization. As one franchise law treatise notes, consolidation maneuvers are routinely framed by courts as "subterfuge" requiring full brand value compensation plus potential punitive damages.⁶

[Cross-reference: State franchise law analysis in Section II.D governs interpretation of "good cause," brand value compensation formulas, and successor manufacturer rights applicable to post-acquisition distributor terminations. Detailed franchise law analysis pending in Section II (not yet completed).]

### B. Standard Distributor Agreement Structure

#### 1. Term and Duration

Beer distributor agreements in the beverage alcohol industry follow standardized structural conventions established over decades of three-tier system practice. **Five-year initial terms** predominate for beer distributors, with automatic renewal clauses that roll agreements forward for successive five-year periods unless either party provides written non-renewal notice (typically 90-180 days before term expiration).⁷ Spirits distributors receive shorter **three-year terms**, reflecting different market dynamics and margin structures in the distilled spirits category.⁸

Certain states impose perpetual duration by statute. **North Carolina's franchise law effectively makes territory assignments irreversible:** once a distribution agreement is executed, the relationship becomes permanent absent "good cause" for termination.⁹ This creates near-insurmountable barriers to consolidation in states with the most protective franchise regimes.

Renewal mechanics typically require affirmative non-renewal notice rather than affirmative renewal elections, placing the burden on the party seeking to exit. For ASBC's 305 distributors operating under automatic renewal structures, ABP inherits ongoing contractual relationships that cannot be terminated at term expiration without satisfying statutory "good cause" requirements in franchise states.

#### 2. Exclusive Territory Grants

Distribution agreements universally grant **exclusive territorial rights** prohibiting suppliers from appointing additional distributors within designated geographic boundaries.¹⁰ Territory definitions employ various methodologies:

- **County boundaries:** Common in rural states (e.g., "exclusive territory comprising Adams, Baker, and Clark Counties, Nebraska")
- **ZIP code clusters:** Prevalent in metropolitan areas (e.g., "ZIP codes 60601-60699" for Chicago downtown)
- **Metropolitan Statistical Areas (MSAs):** Federal census definitions for major urban centers
- **State borders:** Entire state assignments for smaller volume states or spirits-only distributors

Many states **mandate exclusive territories by statute**, imposing substantial penalties on wholesalers for deliveries outside designated geographic areas.¹¹ These statutory exclusivity requirements create a zero-sum game: appointing a second distributor within an existing territory necessitates termination of the incumbent—which in turn triggers "good cause" and brand value compensation requirements in franchise states.

**Consolidation Barrier:** ABP cannot implement a hub-and-spoke network model by overlaying preferred distributors onto existing territories. The exclusive territory system requires sequential termination-then-appointment, exposing ABP to full statutory compensation obligations for each terminated distributor.

#### 3. Performance Requirements and Enforceability Standards

Typical distributor agreements impose quantifiable performance obligations including:¹²

- **Minimum sales targets:** Annual volume commitments (cases, barrels, or dollar-denominated)
- **On-time delivery percentages:** Industry standard of 95%+ delivery within promised timeframes
- **Sales staff minimums:** Required number of dedicated sales representatives assigned to supplier's brands
- **Market coverage:** Geographic penetration requirements (e.g., "maintain distribution in 80% of licensed retail accounts within territory")
- **Warehouse standards:** Temperature control, storage capacity, handling procedures for product quality maintenance

However, franchise law states impose strict **commercially reasonable standards** that override contractual performance metrics. Texas's definitional framework typifies this statutory overlay:

> "Good cause" means the failure by any party to an agreement, without reasonable excuse or justification, to comply substantially with an **essential, reasonable, and commercially acceptable requirement** imposed by the other party under the terms of an agreement.¹³

This four-part test places heavy burdens on suppliers seeking to terminate for performance deficiencies:

**Element 1: Essential**—The requirement must be material to the supplier's business objectives, not merely aspirational or convenience-based.

**Element 2: Reasonable**—The metric must be achievable by a competent distributor operating in comparable market conditions. Targets that prove unattainable due to category decline, supply chain disruptions, or force majeure events fail this test.¹⁴

**Element 3: Commercially Acceptable**—Industry standards govern. If 95% on-time delivery is standard across the distribution tier, requiring 99% may be deemed commercially unacceptable absent justification.

**Element 4: Non-Discriminatory**—Suppliers must apply standards uniformly to similarly situated distributors. Selective enforcement against a single distributor while tolerating identical underperformance by others vitiates "good cause."¹⁵

**COVID-19 Force Majeure Precedents:** Recent litigation established that distributors can invoke force majeure and commercial impracticability defenses when performance failures result from pandemic-related disruptions.¹⁶ Courts require suppliers to prove causation between distributor's actions (or inactions) and performance deficiencies, rejecting arguments that economic hardship alone excuses supplier's obligations.¹⁷

**Texas Lone Star Hypothetical Application:**

Consider a distributor achieving 88% on-time delivery (required: 95%), maintaining 5 sales representatives (required: 8), and missing annual sales targets by 12%. Franchise law analysis suggests:

- **On-time delivery:** Supplier prevails **only if** comparable distributors are meeting the 95% standard; distributor prevails if driver shortages or supply chain disruptions affected entire market. Outcome: **fact-intensive litigation risk.**

- **Sales staff:** Supplier prevails **only if** other distributors in comparable territories maintain 8+ representatives; distributor prevails if labor market conditions made hiring impossible at budgeted compensation levels. Outcome: **fact-intensive litigation risk.**

- **Sales targets:** Distributor **likely prevails** if craft beer category declined 8% nationally during the measurement period, rendering the target commercially unreasonable. Suppliers bear duty to adjust targets in good faith when market conditions deteriorate. Outcome: **distributor advantage.**

**Overall Assessment:** Performance-based terminations carry **10-20% litigation rates**, with franchise law disputes typically settling for 30-50% of brand value to avoid protracted court proceedings.¹⁸

See Section V.E (Joint Employer Analysis), supra at ¶¶ 281-312, analyzing wage/hour compliance issues and potential joint employer liability if distributors misclassify sales staff.

#### 4. Marketing Support Obligations

Distribution agreements impose mutual marketing obligations creating ongoing financial commitments:

**Supplier Obligations:**¹⁹
- Annual marketing fund contributions ($5M-$8M estimated aggregate across 305 distributors based on industry norms of $0.50-$1.50 per case)
- Point-of-sale materials (branded displays, signage, promotional items)
- Brand manager support (dedicated personnel assigned to top distributors)
- Training programs (product knowledge, compliance, sales techniques)
- Co-op advertising (shared costs for local market campaigns)

**Distributor Obligations:**
- Deployment of marketing materials throughout territory
- Maintenance of brand representation standards
- Participation in promotional activities and retail events
- Provision of sales reports and market intelligence to supplier

These marketing commitments represent **sunk costs that cannot be recovered upon termination,** strengthening distributors' equitable claims to brand value compensation. Courts consider marketing investments when calculating "goodwill" components of termination payments.²⁰

### C. Franchise Law Override of Contractual Provisions (CRITICAL)

#### 1. Void Contract Provisions

The following distributor agreement terms are **VOID and unenforceable** in the 25+ states with beer franchise laws, regardless of contract language:

**A. "Terminate at Will" Clauses**

Contractual language such as "Either party may terminate this agreement at any time for any reason with 30 days' notice" is superseded by statutory "good cause" requirements.²¹ Texas's franchise statute declares: "The effect of this Act may not be varied by agreement."²² Courts uniformly hold that freedom-of-contract principles yield to franchise law's protective regime.

**B. "No Compensation" Provisions**

Clauses stating "Upon termination, distributor not entitled to compensation for brand value, goodwill, or distribution rights" are void in franchise states that mandate compensation calculated via gross profit multiples or fair market value methodologies.²³

**C. Waivers of Statutory Rights**

Agreements containing "Distributor hereby waives all rights under [State] franchise laws" are unenforceable as against public policy.²⁴ Franchise protections are considered non-waivable to prevent suppliers from extracting contractual surrenders of statutory entitlements through superior bargaining power.

**D. Shortened Notice Periods**

Contractual 30-day or 60-day termination notice requirements are extended to the statutory minimum—typically **90 days' notice plus 60-day cure period**—yielding effective 150-day minimum termination timelines.²⁵

**E. Unilateral Performance Modification Rights**

Provisions permitting suppliers to "change performance requirements at any time in sole discretion" are constrained by the "reasonable, material, non-discriminatory" standards discussed above. Suppliers cannot unilaterally impose commercially unreasonable targets and then terminate for non-compliance.²⁶

**Application to ASBC:** Any distributor agreements containing these void provisions provide false comfort regarding ABP's post-acquisition termination flexibility. The **statutory framework controls, not the contract text.**

#### 2. State-Specific Carve-Outs and Successor Manufacturer Rights

While franchise law states generally impose uniform "good cause" and compensation requirements, critical state variations create strategic opportunities:

**California's Unique Framework:** Unlike typical franchise states, California does **not** require "good cause" for termination. However, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25000.7 prohibits termination "solely based on failure to meet sales goals or quotas that are not commercially reasonable under prevailing market conditions."²⁷ This nuanced approach provides greater supplier flexibility than blanket "good cause" regimes while still protecting distributors from arbitrary performance standards. Additionally, § 25000.2 requires successor manufacturers to compensate wholesalers at fair market value upon termination.²⁸

**New York's Craft Brewery Carve-Out:** N.Y. Alco. Bev. Control Law creates exemptions for breweries producing <300,000 barrels annually **and** making up ≤3% of any distributor's annual sales.²⁹ Qualifying craft breweries can terminate without "good cause" by paying fair market value of distribution rights lost.

**Critical Limitation:** If ASBC's production exceeds 300,000 barrels/year (data requires verification) **or** ASBC brands represent >3% of any distributor's sales volume, this carve-out does **not** apply and standard "good cause" requirements control.³⁰

**Ohio's Successor Manufacturer Exception (STRATEGIC ADVANTAGE):**

Ohio Rev. Code § 1333.851 provides ABP with **statutory termination rights unavailable in other franchise states:**³¹

> A successor manufacturer may give written notice of termination **within 90 days** of merger, acquisition, or reorganization. The parties shall negotiate in good faith to determine the diminished value of the distributor's business. If unable to resolve within 90 days of notice, either party may file a court action, and the court shall determine diminished value within 90 days of filing.³²

**Strategic Value:** This exception bypasses the "good cause" burden entirely, providing a predictable statutory procedure with fixed timelines. ABP can terminate ASBC's Ohio distributors within the 90-day post-closing window by providing notice and paying court-determined diminished value—eliminating litigation risk over whether termination justification satisfies "good cause."³³

**Estimated Impact:** If ASBC maintains 15-25 distributor relationships in Ohio, § 1333.851 enables consolidation of these accounts at estimated cost of $7.5M-$37.5M (assuming $500K-$1.5M brand value per distributor). This represents one of the few consolidation opportunities available without protracted "good cause" litigation.

**Wisconsin's Brand Compensation Mandate:** When a brewer terminates, cancels, or fails to renew an agreement, the **successor wholesaler chosen by the brewer** must pay the terminated wholesaler fair market value of distribution rights.³⁴ This unique "successor pays" structure shifts compensation obligations from supplier to the incoming distributor, though suppliers typically negotiate economic responsibility in side agreements.

#### 3. Brand Value Compensation Formulas and Exposure Calculation

Franchise statutes mandate compensation upon termination but rarely specify calculation methodologies, leaving formulas to common law development and contractual negotiation. The **gross profit multiple method** predominates:³⁵

```
Brand Value = (Average Annual Gross Profit over 3 years) × Multiplier

Where:
- Gross Profit = Net sales revenue - Cost of goods sold (excluding distribution costs)
- Multiplier = 2.0x to 4.0x based on growth trajectory, product category, market position
```

**Multiplier Factors:**
- **4.0x:** High-growth craft brands with 15%+ annual volume increases
- **3.0-3.5x:** Moderate-growth established brands in stable markets
- **2.0-2.5x:** Mature or declining brands with static/negative volume trends

**Illustrative Calculation (Mid-Tier Distributor):**

- Year 1 gross profit from ASBC brands: $450,000
- Year 2 gross profit: $500,000
- Year 3 gross profit: $550,000
- Three-year average: **$500,000**
- Multiplier (moderate growth craft portfolio): **3.0x**
- **Brand Value = $1,500,000**

**Aggregate Exposure Analysis:**

Applying industry-standard gross profit margins (18-25% for beer, 25-35% for spirits) and volume-weighted multipliers to ASBC's 305-distributor network:³⁶

| Distributor Tier | Count | Avg. Annual Gross Profit | Multiplier | Per-Distributor Value | Subtotal Exposure |
|------------------|-------|-------------------------|------------|----------------------|------------------|
| Major (Top 10) | 10 | $2,000,000 | 3.5x | $7,000,000 | $70,000,000 |
| Mid-Tier (11-60) | 50 | $500,000 | 3.0x | $1,500,000 | $75,000,000 |
| Small (61-305) | 245 | $100,000 | 2.5x | $250,000 | $61,250,000 |
| **TOTAL** | **305** | — | — | — | **$206,250,000** |

**Spirits Distributor Offset:** Approximately 125 spirits distributors operate under weaker franchise protections (only 10-15 states extend franchise laws to distilled spirits vs. 25+ for beer).³⁷ Estimated reduction: $40M-$60M if spirits distributors face no statutory compensation obligations in non-franchise jurisdictions.

**Net Beer Distributor Exposure:** $140M-$180M for 180 beer distributors in franchise states.

**Risk Mitigation Considerations:**
- Actual ASBC distributor profitability data (not available for this analysis) may yield materially different exposure
- Negotiated contractual formulas with caps or floors override gross profit multiple defaults
- Non-franchise state distributors (estimated 30-40% of spirits distributors) may have minimal compensation entitlements
- Voluntary exit buyout packages negotiated at 50-70% of brand value reduce litigation costs

See Section IV.F (Financial Impact), supra at ¶¶ 387-446, for analysis of how brand value compensation exposure affects purchase price valuation with recommended $150M-$250M discount for immediate consolidation strategy versus $30M-$120M for "Grow Into" strategy.

### D. Change of Control Provisions—Transaction-Critical Risk

#### 1. Categories of Anti-Assignment and Deemed Assignment Clauses

Distributor agreements contain varying levels of change-of-control protections, creating 183-244 separate consent requirements for ABP's acquisition:

**Type 1: Basic Anti-Assignment (Ambiguous Application)**

> "Neither party may assign this Agreement without the prior written consent of the other party."³⁸

**Legal Issue:** Courts split on whether stock sales or mergers constitute "assignment" under basic anti-assignment language. Some jurisdictions hold that change of corporate ownership via stock transfer is not an "assignment" of contract rights requiring consent.³⁹ Others treat any change of control as assignment "by operation of law."⁴⁰ **Estimated prevalence:** 30-40% of ASBC agreements likely contain basic anti-assignment clauses with ambiguous application to ABP's acquisition structure.

**Type 2: Comprehensive Deemed Assignment (Directly Triggered)**

> "Any change of control of Supplier resulting from merger, consolidation, stock sale, or asset sale shall be deemed an assignment requiring Distributor's prior written consent. 'Change of control' means any transaction resulting in a change of 50% or more of Supplier's voting equity."⁴¹

**Legal Effect:** This language **unambiguously captures ABP's acquisition** of ASBC. Courts enforce comprehensive deemed assignment clauses, treating change-of-control transactions as equivalent to direct assignment.⁴² Failure to obtain distributor consent constitutes material breach. **Estimated prevalence:** 30-50% of agreements likely contain comprehensive deemed assignment provisions based on industry standard form practices.

**Type 3: Unilateral Termination Right (Distributor Option)**

> "Distributor may terminate this Agreement upon 30 days' notice if Supplier undergoes a change of control or is acquired by a competitor of Distributor's existing suppliers."⁴³

**Legal Effect:** Provides distributor **unilateral exit right** upon ABP acquisition. No consent negotiation—distributor can simply terminate. In franchise states, termination may trigger brand value compensation obligations even though distributor initiated exit.⁴⁴ **Estimated prevalence:** 10-20% of agreements likely include distributor termination options.

**Aggregate Consent Burden:** Assuming 60-80% of 305 agreements contain some form of change-of-control provision, ABP faces **183-244 separate consent requirements.** Even if ABP's legal position is that certain basic anti-assignment clauses do not apply to stock acquisitions, distributors will assert consent rights, forcing litigation or negotiated resolutions.

#### 2. Statutory Asymmetry: Distributor Transfer Rights vs. Supplier Constraints

Franchise laws create **asymmetric change-of-control treatment** favoring distributors:

**Distributor-Initiated Transfers:** Most franchise states prohibit suppliers from unreasonably withholding consent to distributor's sale or transfer of distribution rights.⁴⁵ North Carolina's statutory framework exemplifies this asymmetry:

> Distributor may sell, assign, or transfer its business to any qualified person. Supplier has 30 days to request additional information and 30 days from receipt to object. **Objection is limited to**: Transferee fails to meet qualifications that are non-discriminatory, material, reasonable, and consistently applied.⁴⁶

**Practical Effect:** Distributors control who acquires their distribution rights; suppliers have minimal veto power limited to bona fide qualification standards. A distributor facing potential termination post-acquisition can sell to a third party, and ABP **cannot block the transfer** unless the buyer is demonstrably unqualified.

**Supplier-Initiated Changes:** Conversely, distributors possess broad consent rights over supplier's M&A transactions. Florida's statutory regime goes further, deeming a supplier's unreasonable withholding of consent to **distributor's** transfer as a "termination" requiring compensation:

> A supplier "terminates" a beer franchise when it denies approval of or unreasonably withholds consent to any assignment, transfer, or sale of a distributor's business assets or stock.⁴⁷

**Strategic Implication:** Distributors are **statutorily empowered** to extract concessions as the price of consenting to ABP's acquisition. Holdout distributors can demand:
- Contract term extensions (additional 2-3 years)
- Performance requirement reductions (10-15% target decreases)
- Marketing support increases ($0.25-$0.50 per case)
- Territory expansions or additional brand assignments
- Most-favored-nation rate parity clauses

#### 3. Pre-Closing Consent Campaign—Strategic Imperative

Given the 183-244 consent requirement estimate, ABP must implement a **tiered pre-closing consent procurement strategy:**⁴⁸

**Tier 1: Top 10 Distributors (72% of Volume)—White-Glove Treatment**

| Distributor | % Volume | Strategy | Estimated Concession Cost |
|-------------|----------|----------|-------------------------|
| RNDC | 18% | C-suite meetings; 3-year extension; territory expansion | $2M-$3M |
| SGWS | 14% | C-suite meetings; enhanced marketing support; rate parity | $1.5M-$2.5M |
| Reyes | 12% | Regional president meetings; performance relief Year 1 | $1.5M-$2M |
| Ben E. Keith | 8% | Regional president meetings; new brand access | $1M-$1.5M |
| Next 6 distributors | 20% | VP-level meetings; standardized consent packages | $4M-$6M |
| **Tier 1 Subtotal** | **72%** | **Dedicated negotiation resources** | **$10M-$15M** |

**Tier 2: Next 40 Distributors (18% of Volume)—Standardized Packages**

Offer uniform consent packages: 1-year contract extension + 5% performance target reduction Year 1 + $0.15/case marketing increase. Estimated cost: $3M-$5M aggregate.

**Tier 3: Remaining 255 Distributors (10% of Volume)—Form Letters**

Mail standardized consent requests with nominal improvements (e.g., ABP commits to honor all existing terms). Many smaller distributors will consent without material concessions. Estimated cost: $2M-$5M for negotiated improvements.

**Total Consent Campaign Budget:** $15M-$25M (1.3-2.1% of purchase price)

**Timeline:** 90-180 days for majority consent procurement. **Closing condition:** All Top 10 distributors consent obtained prior to close.

**Refusal Scenarios and Remedies:**

If 10-20% of distributors refuse consent (18-49 distributors), ABP faces three options:

**Option A: Litigate Breach of Contract Claims**
Argue that anti-assignment clauses do not apply to stock acquisitions or that refusal was unreasonable. Timeline: 18-36 months. Cost: $300K-$500K per case. Risk: Courts may issue preliminary injunctions blocking closing.

**Option B: Negotiate Improved Terms**
Offer enhanced concessions beyond standard packages. Incremental cost: $200K-$500K per holdout distributor = $3.6M-$24.5M.

**Option C: Terminate Holdout Distributors Pre-Closing**
ASBC terminates non-consenting distributors prior to ABP acquisition closing (with ASBC bearing compensation costs). This "cleans up" the distributor base but requires ASBC financial resources and exposes ASBC to litigation risk during the interim period. Typically feasible only for smallest distributors.

See Section IV.D.3 (Pre-Closing Consent Campaign), supra at ¶¶ 252-290, analyzing distributor consent procurement requirements and 90-180 day consent campaign timeline affecting transaction closing.

### E. Distributor Consolidation Strategy Analysis—Economic Feasibility Assessment

#### 1. ABP's Strategic Objective and Business Rationale

Industry consolidation trends strongly favor **fewer, larger distributors** operating hub-and-spoke networks with fully scaled operations.⁴⁹ ABP's stated goal—consolidating ASBC's 305 distributors to 50 "strategic partners"—aligns with consolidation strategies pursued by major suppliers (AB InBev, Molson Coors, Constellation Brands) seeking:

- Distribution cost reduction: 10-15% savings through route optimization and overhead elimination
- Improved market coverage: Larger distributors provide better retail penetration
- Marketing efficiency: Coordinated programs with fewer partners reduce complexity
- Portfolio integration: Combined ABP + ASBC brand distribution through unified channel

Historical precedent: Constellation Brands terminated four California wholesalers in 2018 to consolidate Mexican beer brands into Reyes network, which had expanded territory through acquisitions.⁵⁰

**However, legal and economic barriers render immediate consolidation commercially infeasible.**

#### 2. Immediate Consolidation Cost-Benefit Analysis (Strategy A)

**Scenario:** ABP terminates 255 distributors (305 → 50) within 24 months post-closing.

| Cost Category | Calculation Method | Estimated Range |
|---------------|-------------------|----------------|
| **Brand value compensation** | 255 distributors × $500K-$1.5M average | $127M-$382M |
| **Litigation costs** | 10% contest (25-26 cases) × $200K-$500K per case | $5M-$13M |
| **Sales disruption** | 15% volume decline × 6-12 months × $87M revenue × 50% territories | $33M-$65M |
| **Retail account attrition** | 15% permanent loss × $87M revenue | $13M (annual; $65M over 5 years) |
| **Management time/legal** | Internal resources over 2-5 year litigation cycle | $5M-$10M |
| **TOTAL COSTS** | | **$183M-$535M** |

| Benefit Category | Calculation Method | Annual Value |
|------------------|-------------------|--------------|
| **Distribution cost savings** | 10-15% reduction on ~$40M distribution spend | $4M-$6M |
| **Improved market coverage** | Incremental revenue from better penetration | $3M-$5M |
| **Marketing efficiency** | Reduced program complexity | $1M-$2M |
| **Portfolio synergies** | ABP + ASBC combined distribution | $2M-$4M |
| **TOTAL ANNUAL BENEFITS** | | **$10M-$17M** |

**Payback Period:** $183M-$535M costs ÷ $10M-$17M annual benefits = **18-54 years**

**Conclusion:** Immediate consolidation strategy produces **unacceptable risk-adjusted returns.** Even under optimistic assumptions (low-end cost, high-end benefit), payback exceeds 10 years—well beyond typical PE hold periods of 5-7 years.

#### 3. "Subterfuge" Doctrine Risk—Punitive Damages Exposure

Courts scrutinize whether "performance" terminations are **pretexts for consolidation.** If terminations cluster immediately post-acquisition and involve distributors previously meeting contractual requirements, courts may find "consolidation as subterfuge" triggering:⁵¹

- Full brand value compensation (no reduction for alleged performance deficiencies)
- **Punitive damages** equal to 2-3x compensatory damages
- Attorneys' fees awards to prevailing distributors
- Injunctive relief blocking further terminations

**Red Flag Indicators:**⁵²
- Timing: Terminations within 6-12 months of acquisition
- Pattern: Multiple terminations across different states simultaneously
- Internal documents: Emails or presentations referencing "consolidation strategy" or "rationalization plan"
- Pre-termination performance: Distributors meeting 80-90% of targets suddenly terminated for "performance"

**Risk Mitigation:** Any termination strategy must be supported by **rigorous, contemporaneous performance documentation** demonstrating legitimate "good cause" unrelated to consolidation objectives. Even then, 10-20% of terminated distributors typically file litigation, forcing costly defense regardless of substantive merits.

#### 4. "Grow Into" Strategy (Strategy B)—Recommended Alternative

Rather than immediate termination-based consolidation, the **"Grow Into" model** redirects resources to preferred partners while allowing natural market forces to consolidate over 5-10 years:⁵³

**Year 1-2: Designation of Strategic Partners**
- Identify 50 distributors as "strategic partners" based on performance, market coverage, financial strength
- **No terminations announced**—all 305 relationships maintained
- New product launches assigned exclusively to strategic partners
- Marketing fund allocations shift toward strategic partners (enhanced support)

**Year 3-5: Selective Consolidation and Natural Attrition**
- Terminate bottom 20% performers (60 distributors) with documented good cause
- Exercise Ohio-style successor manufacturer statutory rights where available (15-25 distributors)
- Natural attrition: 15-20% of non-strategic distributors retire, sell businesses voluntarily, or exit due to declining volume⁵⁴
- **Estimated cost:** $30M-$90M compensation over 3-5 years

**Year 6-10: Market-Driven Consolidation Completion**
- Strategic partners acquire non-strategic distributors through market transactions (ABP not directly involved)
- Remaining non-strategic distributors operate with static/declining volume as strategic partners capture growth
- Performance-based terminations of chronic underperformers (documented deficiencies)
- **Estimated cost:** $0-$30M additional compensation

**Total "Grow Into" Strategy Cost:** $30M-$120M over 10 years

**Cost Savings vs. Immediate Consolidation:** $63M-$415M (net savings of 34-78%)

**Payback Period:** 1.8-12 years (median: 4.5 years) vs. 18-54 years for immediate consolidation

**Additional Strategic Advantages:**
- Minimal litigation risk (natural attrition requires no terminations)
- No "consolidation as subterfuge" exposure (market forces drive outcomes)
- Preservation of distributor relationships during transition (avoids antagonizing entire network)
- Flexibility to adjust strategic partner designations based on performance evolution

**Recommendation:** ABP should **abandon immediate consolidation strategy** and adopt "Grow Into" model as official post-acquisition integration plan. This single strategic decision reduces quantified risk exposure by **$110M-$282M in expected value terms** (probability-weighted).⁵⁵

See Financial Impact Analysis § II.D (Distributor Network Risk), at pp. 89-127, for analysis of how "Grow Into" strategy eliminates $140M-$382M gross exposure and reduces expected losses by 79-85%.

### F. Financial Impact and Purchase Price Recommendations

#### 1. Quantified Risk Exposure Summary

| Risk Finding | Severity | Probability | Gross Exposure | Weighted Impact | Recommended Mitigation |
|--------------|----------|-------------|----------------|-----------------|----------------------|
| **Immediate Consolidation (If Pursued)** | CRITICAL | 100% if attempted | $183M-$535M | $183M-$535M | ✗ **ABANDON STRATEGY** |
| **"Grow Into" Alternative** | HIGH | 30-50% over 10 years | $30M-$120M | $9M-$60M | ✓ **ADOPT AS PRIMARY PLAN** |
| **Change-of-Control Consents** | HIGH | 60-80% require consent | $15M-$25M procurement costs | $12M-$20M | Pre-closing consent campaign (Top 50 distributors) |
| **Brand Value Compensation (Unavoidable)** | MEDIUM | 100% for any terminations | $500K-$1.5M per distributor | Context-dependent | Negotiate voluntary exits at 50-70% of formula value |
| **Distributor Litigation** | MEDIUM | 10-20% of terminations | $5M-$13M legal + settlements | $500K-$2.6M annually | Performance documentation; offer settlements at 30-50% brand value |
| **Spirits Distributor Consolidation** | LOW | Weaker protections | $30M-$60M (if consolidated Year 1-2) | $9M-$30M | Prioritize spirits over beer for early consolidation |

#### 2. Risk Impact Table with Purchase Price Implications

| Finding | Gross Exposure | Probability | Weighted Impact | Recommendation |
|---------|---------------|-------------|-----------------|----------------|
| **Immediate Consolidation (Strategy A)** | $183M-$535M | 100% if pursued | $183M-$535M | **Abandon; adopt Strategy B** |
| **"Grow Into" Strategy (Strategy B)** | $30M-$120M | 30-50% materialization | $9M-$60M | **Primary consolidation approach** |
| **Differential (Strategy A vs. B)** | **$153M-$415M** | — | **$174M-$475M net savings** | **Critical strategic decision** |
| **Change-of-Control Consent Procurement** | $15M-$25M | 60-80% of 305 agreements | $12M-$20M | Pre-closing consent campaign targeting Top 50 distributors (72% volume) |
| **Litigation Reserve (5-year horizon)** | $5M-$13M | 10-20% of terminations | $1M-$2.6M annually | Budget $5M-$10M outside counsel over 5 years |

**Aggregate Expected Impact:** Adopting "Grow Into" strategy + pre-closing consent campaign reduces distributor-related exposure from $198M-$560M (immediate consolidation) to $26M-$90M (phased approach)—a **$172M-$470M reduction** in expected costs.⁵⁶

#### 3. Purchase Price Adjustment Recommendations

Based on distributor-related risks, this analysis recommends the following transaction structure adjustments:⁵⁷

**Purchase Price Considerations:**

The $1.2B purchase price should reflect distributor network complexities through one of three mechanisms:

**Option A: Direct Price Reduction**
Reduce purchase price by $50M-$100M (4-8%) to reflect:
- Change-of-control consent procurement costs ($15M-$25M)
- "Grow Into" strategy implementation costs over 5 years ($30M-$60M)
- Litigation reserve for contested terminations ($5M-$15M)

**Option B: Enhanced Escrow Without Price Reduction**
Maintain $1.2B purchase price but increase escrow to $126M (10.5%) with:
- $72M general indemnity escrow (18 months)
- $24M tax/regulatory escrow (36 months)
- $18M employment escrow (24 months)
- $12M litigation escrow (18 months)

Distributor-specific representations and warranties would include:
- Schedule lists all 305 distributor agreements with change-of-control provision identification
- No pending/threatened distributor litigation or termination disputes
- No distributor terminations in past 3 years without "good cause" documentation
- Disclosure of any distributors currently in breach or subject to performance improvement plans

**Option C: Hybrid Approach (Recommended)**
$25M-$50M purchase price reduction + $105M escrow (8.75%) combining:
- Risk-adjusted pricing reflecting known distributor complexities
- Adequate escrow protection for unknown exposures
- Seller receives higher net cash at closing compared to Option B ($1,095M vs. $1,074M)

**Rationale:** Hybrid approach balances competitive purchase price maintenance (important in auction processes) with appropriate risk allocation. The $105M escrow provides **7.6x-16.4x coverage** of median expected distributor-related losses under "Grow Into" strategy.⁵⁸

#### 4. Specific Indemnification Language—Distributor Representations

The following distributor-specific representations should be included in the Purchase Agreement:

**Section X.1—Distributor Agreements Schedule:**

> Seller has delivered to Buyer a complete and accurate schedule (Schedule X.1) listing all distributor agreements to which the Company is a party, including: (a) distributor name and principal place of business; (b) products covered; (c) territory description; (d) term and expiration date; (e) presence and type of change-of-control consent provisions; (f) performance requirements and current compliance status; and (g) brand value compensation formula (if specified).

**Section X.2—Distributor Agreement Enforceability:**

> Each distributor agreement listed on Schedule X.1 is in full force and effect and constitutes a legal, valid, and binding obligation of the parties thereto. The Company is not in material breach of any distributor agreement, and to Seller's Knowledge, no distributor is in material breach of its obligations under any distributor agreement. No distributor has provided written notice of termination, breach, or intent not to renew.

**Section X.3—Distributor Litigation and Disputes:**

> There is no pending or, to Seller's Knowledge, threatened litigation, arbitration, or administrative proceeding involving any distributor relationship. The Company has not received any demand letters, complaints, or notices of dispute from any distributor within the past three (3) years. No distributor has filed a complaint with any state alcohol beverage control agency concerning the Company's conduct.

**Section X.4—Change-of-Control Consent Requirements:**

> Schedule X.4 identifies all distributor agreements containing provisions requiring distributor consent to change of control of Supplier or granting distributor termination rights upon change of control. Seller shall use commercially reasonable efforts to obtain all such consents prior to Closing, including consents from the Top 10 distributors identified on Schedule X.4. **Closing Condition:** Consents from all Top 10 distributors shall be a condition precedent to Buyer's obligation to close.

**Section X.5—Performance Terminations:**

> The Company has not terminated any distributor agreement within the past three (3) years except for (a) terminations identified on Schedule X.5 with accompanying documentation of "good cause" as defined under applicable state franchise law, or (b) voluntary distributor exits by mutual agreement. All terminated distributors received compensation as required by applicable law or contractual provisions.

**Indemnification Cap:** $50M-$100M (10-20% of purchase price) for breaches of distributor-related representations, with 3-5 year survival period (extended beyond standard 18-24 month survival for general representations given long-tail nature of distributor disputes).

### G. Post-Closing Action Plan—Distributor Management Strategy

#### 1. Days 1-30: Relationship Stabilization

**Action Item 1:** Distribute "Welcome Letter" to all 305 distributors within 48 hours of closing signed by ABP CEO, stating:
- ABP's commitment to existing distributor relationships
- No immediate changes to agreement terms or territory assignments
- Introduction of ABP distributor relations leadership team
- Invitation to scheduled regional distributor meetings

**Action Item 2:** Implement **distributor consolidation freeze** policy memo circulated to all ABP sales, operations, and strategy personnel:

> **POLICY DIRECTIVE—EFFECTIVE IMMEDIATELY:** No distributor termination notices, territory reassignments, or consolidation proposals shall be communicated to any ASBC distributor without prior written approval of General Counsel and Chief Commercial Officer. All distributor communications must emphasize continuity and partnership.

**Action Item 3:** Complete distributor agreement audit identifying:
- All 183-244 agreements with change-of-control provisions requiring consent
- Distributors currently in breach or subject to performance disputes
- Agreements expiring within 12 months requiring renewal negotiations
- Opportunities for voluntary exit negotiations (distributors nearing retirement, financial distress indicators)

**Budget:** $50K-$100K for external counsel review of agreements; $25K for distributor relations technology platform implementation

#### 2. Days 31-90: Consent Procurement and Strategic Partner Designation

**Action Item 4:** Execute pre-closing consent campaign targeting remaining distributors (assuming Top 10 consented as closing condition):

- Send consent packages to Next 40 distributors (Tier 2) offering standardized improvements
- Mail form consent requests to remaining 255 distributors (Tier 3)
- Track consent receipt; follow up with non-responders at 30-day intervals
- **Goal:** 150+ consents obtained within 90 days post-closing

**Action Item 5:** Designate 50 strategic partners based on multi-factor assessment:
- Performance metrics (sales growth, on-time delivery, market coverage)
- Financial strength (credit rating, balance sheet analysis)
- Geographic footprint (coverage of key markets)
- Alignment with ABP's existing distributor relationships (minimize conflicts)

Communicate strategic partner designation via personalized letter from ABP CEO outlining:
- Enhanced marketing support and new product access
- Partnership in long-term growth strategy
- Invitation to annual strategic partner summit

**Budget:** $100K-$200K for consent procurement negotiations; $50K for strategic partner assessment consulting

#### 3. Months 4-12: Performance Management System Implementation

**Action Item 6:** Implement data-driven performance management system for all 305 distributors:

**Quarterly Performance Scorecards:**
- Sales volume vs. target (adjusted for market conditions using Nielsen/IRI data)
- On-time delivery percentage
- Market coverage (retail account penetration)
- Brand representation (compliance with display standards)
- Peer comparison (performance vs. other distributors in comparable markets)

**Performance Improvement Plans (PIPs):** For distributors scoring in bottom 20%:
- 90-day PIP with specific improvement targets
- ABP support resources (additional marketing funds, training, dedicated brand manager)
- Monthly progress reviews with documented feedback
- Cure opportunities before any termination consideration

**Documentation Protocol:** Maintain meticulous records of all distributor communications, performance data, market condition analyses, and cure efforts—essential for establishing "good cause" in any subsequent termination litigation.

**Budget:** $150K-$300K for performance management software platform; $200K annually for dedicated distributor relations personnel

#### 4. Years 2-3: Selective Consolidation Execution

**Action Item 7:** Execute bottom 20% performance-based terminations (60 distributors) with documented good cause:

**Termination Procedure:**
- 90-day written notice specifying all deficiencies with supporting data
- 60-day cure period with offer of ABP support resources
- If deficiencies not cured, proceed with termination and brand value compensation negotiation
- Offer voluntary exit buyouts at 50-70% of formula value to avoid litigation

**Action Item 8:** Exercise Ohio successor manufacturer rights for 15-25 Ohio distributors:
- Issue 90-day statutory termination notice within post-closing window
- Negotiate diminished value in good faith
- If no agreement within 90 days, file court action for judicial determination
- Pay court-determined amount and complete transition

**Action Item 9:** Spirits distributor consolidation priority:
- Focus consolidation efforts on 125 spirits distributors operating in non-franchise states
- Leverage weaker statutory protections (no "good cause" requirements in 35-40 states)
- Target consolidation of 30-50 spirits distributors in Year 2-3
- Estimated cost: $15M-$30M vs. $45M-$75M for equivalent beer distributor consolidations

**Budget:** $30M-$90M brand value compensation; $5M-$10M litigation costs (assuming 10-15% contest rate)

#### 5. Years 4-10: Natural Market Consolidation

**Action Item 10:** Maintain "Grow Into" strategy discipline:

- **No further proactive terminations** except documented good cause performance failures
- Continue preferential resource allocation to 50 strategic partners
- Support strategic partners' acquisition of non-strategic distributors (market-driven transactions)
- Allow natural attrition through retirement, voluntary exits, business sales

**Monitoring Metrics:**
- Annual distributor count reduction tracking (goal: 10-15 distributors per year through natural attrition)
- Strategic partner volume concentration (goal: 85%+ volume through 50 partners by Year 10)
- Litigation rate (goal: <5% of all distributor separations result in filed litigation)
- Cost per distributor exit (goal: <$400K average including compensation and legal fees)

**Expected Outcome by Year 10:**
- Total distributor count: 100-150 (reduction from 305)
- Strategic partners: 50 (achieving 85%+ volume concentration)
- Total consolidation cost: $30M-$120M vs. $183M-$535M immediate consolidation alternative
- Net savings: $63M-$415M achieved through patient, strategic approach

### H. Conclusion: Strategic Imperatives for Distributor Network Management

The comprehensive analysis of ASBC's 305-distributor network reveals five critical findings that must guide ABP's post-acquisition integration strategy:

**Finding 1: Immediate Consolidation Is Commercially Infeasible**

The $183M-$535M cost of consolidating from 305 to 50 distributors within 2-3 years produces unacceptable payback periods of 18-54 years. Franchise law requirements for "good cause," brand value compensation, and extended termination timelines (90-day notice + 60-day cure) combine with 10-20% litigation rates to render aggressive consolidation economically irrational.⁵⁹

**Finding 2: "Grow Into" Strategy Delivers Superior Risk-Adjusted Returns**

Phasing consolidation over 5-10 years through strategic resource allocation, natural attrition, and market-driven transactions reduces costs by 34-78% ($63M-$415M savings) while achieving comparable long-term outcomes. This patient approach avoids "consolidation as subterfuge" litigation risk and preserves critical distributor relationships during the transition period.⁶⁰

**Finding 3: Change-of-Control Consent Risk Is Transaction-Critical**

With 60-80% of agreements likely containing some form of consent requirement, ABP faces 183-244 separate consent negotiations. The Top 10 distributors (72% of volume) must provide unconditional consent as a **mandatory closing condition**—any alternative exposes ABP to material business continuity risk if major distribution partners exit post-closing.⁶¹

**Finding 4: Spirits Distributors Offer Consolidation Opportunity**

The 125 spirits distributors operate under materially weaker franchise protections (only 10-15 states extend franchise laws to spirits vs. 25+ for beer), enabling targeted consolidation at one-third the cost of equivalent beer distributor terminations. **Recommendation:** Prioritize spirits distributor consolidation in Years 2-3 post-closing while maintaining stability in the beer distribution network.⁶²

**Finding 5: Ohio Successor Manufacturer Exception Provides Tactical Advantage**

Ohio Rev. Code § 1333.851's statutory termination procedure allows ABP to exit 15-25 Ohio distributor relationships within 90 days of closing by paying court-determined diminished value—bypassing "good cause" litigation entirely. This rare exception should be exercised promptly within the statutory 90-day window.⁶³

**Ultimate Recommendation:** ABP should **formally abandon immediate consolidation strategy,** adopt "Grow Into" model as official integration policy, budget $30M-$120M for phased consolidation over 10 years, and prioritize pre-closing consent procurement from Top 50 distributors representing 80%+ of volume. This strategic pivot eliminates $110M-$282M of quantified risk exposure while achieving long-term distribution network optimization objectives.

29. N.Y. ALCO. BEV. CONTROL LAW (2012 craft brewery reform); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 538-543 (breweries <300,000 barrels/year making up ≤3% of distributor's sales can terminate without "good cause" by paying fair market value).

30. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 546 ("If ASBC's beer production exceeds 300,000 barrels/year OR represents >3% of any distributor's sales, this carve-out DOES NOT apply → standard 'good cause' requirement controls").

31. OHIO REV. CODE § 1333.851 (Distributors of Acquired Manufacturers); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 549-558.

32. *Id.* § 1333.851(B) (successor manufacturer may terminate within 90 days of acquisition; parties negotiate diminished value for 90 days; court determines value if no resolution).

33. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 557 ("Ohio statute gives ABP (as successor manufacturer) the RIGHT to terminate ASBC's distributors in Ohio within 90 days of acquisition closing, provided ABP pays diminished value. This is an exception to typical 'good cause' requirements").

34. WIS. STAT. § 135.03 (brand compensation law); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 559-565 (successor wholesaler chosen by brewer must pay terminated wholesaler fair market value).

35. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 592-617 (gross profit multiple method predominates: Brand Value = Average Annual Gross Profit × 2.0-4.0 multiplier); *How to Value a Distribution Business: Multiples Cash Flow*, MID MARKET BUSINESSES (discussing industry valuation practices).

36. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 661-675 (aggregate exposure calculation applying gross profit margins of 18-25% for beer and 25-35% for spirits with volume-weighted multipliers).

37. *Id.* at 393-406, 127-134 (documenting that only 10-15 states extend franchise laws to spirits vs. 25+ for beer; spirits distributors face "fewer statutory protections," "greater termination flexibility," "lower compensation obligations").

38. *Id.* at 682-688 (basic anti-assignment clause language and legal ambiguity); *Anti-Assignment Clauses in Commercial Contracts*, OLENDER FELDMAN LLP (courts split on whether stock sales constitute "assignment").

39. *Id.* at 686 (noting jurisdictional split: some courts hold stock transfer is not "assignment" of contract rights; others treat change of control as assignment "by operation of law").

40. *How Assignment Clauses Can Affect M&A*, ACC DOCKET (Nov. 2022) (discussing interpretation of "assignment by operation of law" in merger contexts).

41. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 689-697 (comprehensive deemed assignment clause directly capturing ABP acquisition); *Don't Confuse Change of Control and Assignment Terms*, TECH CONTRACTS (Sept. 11, 2020).

42. *Id.* at 695-696 ("Courts generally enforce comprehensive anti-assignment clauses"; failure to obtain consent constitutes material breach).

43. *Id.* at 699-706 (termination right upon change of control gives distributor unilateral exit option).

44. *Id.* at 705 (noting franchise states may require brand value compensation even though distributor initiated termination via change-of-control option exercise).

45. *Id.* at 962-980 (most franchise states prohibit suppliers from unreasonably withholding consent to distributor's transfer); CAL. BUS. & PROF. CODE § 25000 (brewer cannot unreasonably withhold consent; liable if denies transfer ability).

46. N.C. GEN. STAT. § 18B-1305 (Beer Franchise Law distributor transfer rights); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 969-975 (distributor may transfer to any qualified person; supplier objection limited to non-discriminatory, material, reasonable qualifications).

47. FLA. STAT. (beer franchise provisions); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 750-756 ("Supplier 'terminates' a beer franchise when it denies approval of or unreasonably withholds consent to any assignment, transfer, or sale of a distributor's business").

48. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 733-760 (recommending tiered pre-closing consent procurement strategy); *Id.* at 1389-1414 (consent campaign recommendations).

49. *Consolidation Helps Beer Distributors Stay Competitive*, TRUIST (discussing hub-and-spoke networks, fully scaled operations); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 913-923 (business rationale for consolidation).

50. *California Family Beer Distributors' Meeting Highlights Effects of Terminations and Consolidation*, BREWBOUND (2018); Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 921 (Constellation Brands 2018 California consolidation to Reyes network).

51. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 937-951 ("Consolidation as subterfuge" doctrine: courts scrutinize whether "performance" termination is pretext; if so, triggers full compensation + potential punitive damages).

52. *Id.* at 946-950 (red flag indicators: timing clustering, pattern of simultaneous terminations, internal "consolidation strategy" documents, pre-termination performance meeting 80-90% of targets).

53. *Id.* at 1099-1175, 1427-1449 ("Grow Into" strategy recommendations: redirect growth to 50 preferred partners, allow natural attrition 5-10 years, avoid mass terminations).

54. *Id.* at 1439 (natural attrition: 15-20% of distributors exit over 5-10 years through retirement, voluntary sales, market forces).

55. Financial Impact Analysis at 136-141, 200-210 (distributor consolidation strategy choice is single largest value driver; immediate consolidation increases expected losses by 10x-12x vs. "Grow Into" strategy; net savings: $110M-$282M).

56. Financial Impact Analysis at 134-142 (quantified risk exposure summary: "Grow Into" + consent campaign reduces distributor exposure from $198M-$560M to $26M-$90M).

57. *Id.* at 518-556, 606-723 (purchase price adjustment scenarios and escrow recommendations).

58. *Id.* at 64 (10.5% escrow provides 2.3x-5.4x coverage of 75th percentile expected losses; $105M enhanced protections escrow provides 7.6x-16.4x coverage of median expected losses under "Grow Into" strategy).

59. Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 1041-1076, 1419-1426 (immediate consolidation cost-benefit analysis: $183M-$535M costs ÷ $10M-$17M annual benefits = 18-54 year payback period).

60. *Id.* at 1099-1175, 1427-1449 ("Grow Into" strategy cost: $30M-$120M over 10 years vs. $183M-$535M immediate consolidation; net savings: $63M-$415M or 34-78%).

61. *Id.* at 763-788, 1389-1414 (change-of-control consent risk: 60-80% of 305 agreements require consent; 183-244 separate negotiations; Top 10 distributors controlling 72% of volume must consent as mandatory closing condition).

62. *Id.* at 126-134, 384-406, 1581-1588 (spirits distributors operate under weaker franchise protections: only 10-15 states extend franchise laws to spirits; "greater termination flexibility"; "lower compensation obligations"; prioritize spirits consolidation Years 2-3).

63. OHIO REV. CODE § 1333.851; Commercial Contracts Analyst Report at 549-558, 1450-1468 (Ohio successor manufacturer exception provides ABP statutory right to terminate within 90 days by paying court-determined diminished value; applies to 15-25 Ohio distributors; estimated cost $7.5M-$37.5M; should be exercised promptly within 90-day statutory window).
